الاحتياج إلى المنطق
وجه تسمية بالمنطق
أما تسميته بالمنطق فلتأثيره في النطق الظاهري، أعني التكلم؛ إذ العارف به يقوى على التكلم بما لا يقوى عليه الجاهل، وكذا في النطق الباطني أعني الإدراك؛ لأن المنطقي يعرف حقائق الأشياء، ويعلم أجناسها وفصولها وأنواعها ولوازمها وخواصها بخلاف الغافل عن هذا العلم الشريف.
وأما تسميته بالميزان؛ فلأنه قسطاس للعقل يوزن به الأفكار الصحيحة، ويعرف به نقصان ما في الأفكار الفاسدة واختلال ما في الأنظار الكاسدة، ومن ثَمّ يقال له: " العلم الآلي"؛ لكونها آلة لجميع العلوم لا سيما للعلوم الحكمية.
تسميته بالمنطق : المنطق إما مصدر ميمي بمعنى النطق، أطلق على هذا الفن مبالغة في مدخليته في تكميل النطق، وإما اسم مكان، كأن هذا العلم محل النطق ومظهره ورسموه بأنه آلة قانونية تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر المنطقي يعرف إلى أنت تعلم أن معرفة حقائق الأشياء ليس في قدرة البشر ، وإنما هو شأن خالق القوى والقدر. قال الشيخ في "التعليقات": نحن لا نعرف من الأشياء إلا الخواص واللوازم، ولا نعرف الفصول المقومة لكل واحد منها الدلالة على حقيقة، بل نعرف أنها أشياء لها خواص وأعراض فإنا لا نعرف حقيقة الأول - يعني الواجب تعالى - ولا العقل ولا النفس ولا الفلك والنار والهواء والماء والأرض ولا نعرف أيضا حقائق الأعراض، مثال ذلك أنا لا نعرف حقيقة الجواهر وإنما عرفنا شيئا له هذه الخاصية، وهو أنه الموجود لا في موضوع، وليس هذا حقيقة ولا نعرف حقيقة الجسم، بل نعرف شيئا له هذه الخواص، وهي الطول والعرض والعمق، إلى آخر ما قال تفصيلا.
قسطاس: القسطاس — بالضم والكسر - الميزان، أو أقوم الموازين، أو هو ميزان العدل، أي ميزان كان كالقسطاس، وهو رومي معرب كذا في "القاموس . العلم الآلي: اعلم أن العلوم إما أن لا تكون في أنفسها آلة لتحصيل شيء آخر، بل كانت مقصودة بذواتها وتسمّى غير آلية وإما أن تكون آلة لتحصيل شيء آخر غير مقصودة في نفسها، وتسمّى آلية، فالمنطق داخل في العلوم الآلية والآلة هي الواسطة بين الفاعل ومنفعله في وصول أثره إليه كالمنشار للنجار، فإنه واسطة بينه وبين الخشب في وصول أثره إليه، فكذا المنطق آلة بين القوة وبين المطالب الكسبية، وتحصيله ليس مقصودا بالذات بل لأنه آلة للعلوم الحكمية بل لسائر العلوم.
The Need for Logic. Reason for naming it Logic: It is named Logic because of its effect on external speech, meaning talking; for the one who knows it is able to speak what the ignorant cannot, and likewise in internal speech, meaning perception; because the logician knows the realities of things, and knows their genera, species, differentiae, properties, and accidents, unlike the heedless of this noble science. As for naming it the Balance, it is because it is a scale for the intellect by which correct thoughts are weighed, and by which the deficiency in corrupt thoughts and the disorder in defective views are known. Hence it is called the Instrumental Science, because it is an instrument for all sciences, especially the philosophical sciences. Its naming as Logic: al-mantiq is either a verbal noun meaning speech, applied to this art as an exaggeration of its role in perfecting speech, or a noun of place, as if this science is the locus and manifestation of speech. They defined it as a legal instrument whose observance safeguards the mind from error in logical thinking. Know that knowledge of the realities of things is not within human power; rather it is the concern of the Creator of powers and capabilities. Al-Shaykh said in al-Ta'liqat: We only know from things the properties and accidents, and we do not know the constitutive differentiae of each indicating its reality; rather we know that they are things with properties and accidents. For we do not know the reality of the First—meaning the Necessary Exalted—nor intellect, nor soul, nor the sphere, fire, air, water, earth; nor do we know the realities of accidents. For example, we do not know the reality of substances; rather we know something with this property: that it exists not in a subject, and this is not its reality. We do not know the reality of body; rather we know something with these properties: length, width, depth. To the end of what he said in detail. Qistas: al-qistas—with damm and kasr—the balance, or the most upright of balances, or it is the balance of justice, any balance like the qistas; it is a Roman word Arabized, as in al-Qamus. The Instrumental Science: Know that sciences are either not in themselves instruments for acquiring something else, but are intended for their own sakes, called non-instrumental; or they are instruments for acquiring something else, not intended in themselves, called instrumental. Logic is among the instrumental sciences. The instrument is the intermediary between the agent and the recipient in conveying its effect, like the saw for the carpenter: it is the intermediary between him and the wood. Similarly, logic is an instrument between the faculty and the acquired objectives. Its acquisition is not intended in itself, but because it is an instrument for the philosophical sciences, nay for all sciences.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage defines logic and explains its various names. The author begins by stating the need for logic, then gives the reason for its name: 'mantiq' (logic) derives from its effect on both external speech (articulation) and internal speech (intellectual perception). The logician, unlike the ignorant, knows the realities, genera, species, and properties of things. The second name, 'al-Mizan' (the Balance), indicates that logic serves as a scale for the intellect to weigh correct thoughts and detect errors in corrupt ones. This leads to its third name, 'al-Ilm al-Aali' (the Instrumental Science), because logic is a tool for all other sciences, especially the philosophical ones. The author then gives a linguistic analysis of the word 'mantiq', noting it could be a verbal noun meaning speech or a noun of place meaning the locus of speech. He then presents the definition of logic: 'a legal instrument whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thinking'. Following this, he emphasizes that full knowledge of the essences of things is beyond human capability, quoting al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) from al-Ta'liqat. The quote explains that we only know properties and accidents, not the constitutive differentiae of things. For example, we know a substance by the property 'exists not in a subject', but this is not its true essence. The passage concludes with notes on the words 'qistas' (a type of balance) and 'al-ilm al-aali', clarifying that instrumental sciences are those sought for the sake of something else, like logic, which is a tool between the intellect and its objectives, analogous to a saw for a carpenter.

Key Terms
المنطق (al-mantiq): In this passage, it is defined as a legal instrument (آلة قانونية) that safeguards the mind from error in thinking; also analyzed etymologically as a verbal noun or noun of place. [Note: No significant shift in the classical tradition.]
الميزان (al-mizan): A name for logic meaning the balance, because it weighs correct and incorrect thoughts. [Note: No significant shift.]
القسطاس (al-qistas): The balance, specifically the most upright balance or the balance of justice; the author explains it as a Roman-origin word Arabized. [Note: No significant shift.]
العلم الآلي (al-ilm al-aali): An instrumental science; logic is called so because it is an instrument for acquiring other sciences, not intended in itself. [Note: No significant shift.]
آلة قانونية (alat qanuniyya): Defined as a legal (or canonical) instrument that safeguards the mind from error in logical thinking. [Note: This phrase is a standard definition of logic in later texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it balances different sciences.

Key Takeaway
Logic is named to emphasize its role in perfecting speech, weighing thoughts, and serving as an instrument for acquiring knowledge.

What Is Mantiq?

The Definition of Logic and Its Limits
Author explains the names of logic and defines it as a legal instrument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the definition of logic as a 'legal instrument' and explain the epistemological limitation that follows.

Core Idea
Logic safeguards the mind but cannot grant knowledge of essences, only properties.

The passage defines logic as a legal instrument that safeguards the mind from error. But it immediately qualifies this by quoting Ibn Sina, who argues that human beings cannot know the true realities (haqiqah) of things; we only know properties and accidents. This lesson examines the definition and its philosophical context.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Find the sentence that defines logic: 'آلة قانونية تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ' (a legal instrument whose observance safeguards the mind from error).
2
Analyze the genus and differentia
The genus is 'instrument', placing logic among tools. The differentia is 'legal' (qanuniyya) and 'safeguards the mind from error'. This distinguishes it from other instruments.
The term 'legal' may refer to general laws or principles.
3
Identify the epistemological limit
The passage then quotes Ibn Sina stating that we cannot know the true essences of things, only their properties. This qualifies the scope of logic: it safeguards from error but does not reveal absolute realities.
4
Consider the implications
Logic is powerful but not omniscient. It provides correctness in reasoning about what we can observe (properties), but the essences of things—especially divine matters—remain beyond its reach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتياج إلى المنطق
وجه تسمية بالمنطق
أما تسميته بالمنطق فلتأثيره في النطق الظاهري، أعني التكلم؛ إذ العارف به يقوى على التكلم بما لا يقوى عليه الجاهل، وكذا في النطق الباطني أعني الإدراك؛ لأن المنطقي يعرف حقائق الأشياء، ويعلم أجناسها وفصولها وأنواعها ولوازمها وخواصها بخلاف الغافل عن هذا العلم الشريف.
وأما تسميته بالميزان؛ فلأنه قسطاس للعقل يوزن به الأفكار الصحيحة، ويعرف به نقصان ما في الأفكار الفاسدة واختلال ما في الأنظار الكاسدة، ومن ثَمّ يقال له: " العلم الآلي"؛ لكونها آلة لجميع العلوم لا سيما للعلوم الحكمية.
تسميته بالمنطق : المنطق إما مصدر ميمي بمعنى النطق، أطلق على هذا الفن مبالغة في مدخليته في تكميل النطق، وإما اسم مكان، كأن هذا العلم محل النطق ومظهره ورسموه بأنه آلة قانونية تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر المنطقي يعرف إلى أنت تعلم أن معرفة حقائق الأشياء ليس في قدرة البشر ، وإنما هو شأن خالق القوى والقدر. قال الشيخ في "التعليقات": نحن لا نعرف من الأشياء إلا الخواص واللوازم، ولا نعرف الفصول المقومة لكل واحد منها الدلالة على حقيقة، بل نعرف أنها أشياء لها خواص وأعراض فإنا لا نعرف حقيقة الأول - يعني الواجب تعالى - ولا العقل ولا النفس ولا الفلك والنار والهواء والماء والأرض ولا نعرف أيضا حقائق الأعراض، مثال ذلك أنا لا نعرف حقيقة الجواهر وإنما عرفنا شيئا له هذه الخاصية، وهو أنه الموجود لا في موضوع، وليس هذا حقيقة ولا نعرف حقيقة الجسم، بل نعرف شيئا له هذه الخواص، وهي الطول والعرض والعمق، إلى آخر ما قال تفصيلا.
قسطاس: القسطاس — بالضم والكسر - الميزان، أو أقوم الموازين، أو هو ميزان العدل، أي ميزان كان كالقسطاس، وهو رومي معرب كذا في "القاموس . العلم الآلي: اعلم أن العلوم إما أن لا تكون في أنفسها آلة لتحصيل شيء آخر، بل كانت مقصودة بذواتها وتسمّى غير آلية وإما أن تكون آلة لتحصيل شيء آخر غير مقصودة في نفسها، وتسمّى آلية، فالمنطق داخل في العلوم الآلية والآلة هي الواسطة بين الفاعل ومنفعله في وصول أثره إليه كالمنشار للنجار، فإنه واسطة بينه وبين الخشب في وصول أثره إليه، فكذا المنطق آلة بين القوة وبين المطالب الكسبية، وتحصيله ليس مقصودا بالذات بل لأنه آلة للعلوم الحكمية بل لسائر العلوم.
The Need for Logic. Reason for naming it Logic: It is named Logic because of its effect on external speech, meaning talking; for the one who knows it is able to speak what the ignorant cannot, and likewise in internal speech, meaning perception; because the logician knows the realities of things, and knows their genera, species, differentiae, properties, and accidents, unlike the heedless of this noble science. As for naming it the Balance, it is because it is a scale for the intellect by which correct thoughts are weighed, and by which the deficiency in corrupt thoughts and the disorder in defective views are known. Hence it is called the Instrumental Science, because it is an instrument for all sciences, especially the philosophical sciences. Its naming as Logic: al-mantiq is either a verbal noun meaning speech, applied to this art as an exaggeration of its role in perfecting speech, or a noun of place, as if this science is the locus and manifestation of speech. They defined it as a legal instrument whose observance safeguards the mind from error in logical thinking. Know that knowledge of the realities of things is not within human power; rather it is the concern of the Creator of powers and capabilities. Al-Shaykh said in al-Ta'liqat: We only know from things the properties and accidents, and we do not know the constitutive differentiae of each indicating its reality; rather we know that they are things with properties and accidents. For we do not know the reality of the First—meaning the Necessary Exalted—nor intellect, nor soul, nor the sphere, fire, air, water, earth; nor do we know the realities of accidents. For example, we do not know the reality of substances; rather we know something with this property: that it exists not in a subject, and this is not its reality. We do not know the reality of body; rather we know something with these properties: length, width, depth. To the end of what he said in detail. Qistas: al-qistas—with damm and kasr—the balance, or the most upright of balances, or it is the balance of justice, any balance like the qistas; it is a Roman word Arabized, as in al-Qamus. The Instrumental Science: Know that sciences are either not in themselves instruments for acquiring something else, but are intended for their own sakes, called non-instrumental; or they are instruments for acquiring something else, not intended in themselves, called instrumental. Logic is among the instrumental sciences. The instrument is the intermediary between the agent and the recipient in conveying its effect, like the saw for the carpenter: it is the intermediary between him and the wood. Similarly, logic is an instrument between the faculty and the acquired objectives. Its acquisition is not intended in itself, but because it is an instrument for the philosophical sciences, nay for all sciences.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner

The Definition of Logic and Its Guardian Role
Defines logic and clarifies the role of observance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between logic itself and the observance of logical laws, and apply the definition to identify what counts as logic.

Core Idea
Logic is a tool; it guards the mind only when observed.

This lesson unpacks the precise definition of logic given by the author. The definition emphasises that logic itself does not automatically protect from error; only its active observance does. Understanding this nuance is essential for properly using logic as an instrument in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition from the text
Locate the phrase: 'المنطق من قوانين تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر'. Note that it says 'مراعاتها' (its observance), not 'هي' (it).
The word 'مراعاتها' is key.
2
Identify the guardian
The author points out that if logic itself guarded the mind, then a logician would never err. Since logicians do err, the guardian must be the active practice of logical rules.
3
Distinguish logic from other sciences
The phrase 'في الفكر' (in thought) excludes sciences like grammar which guard speech. Logic uniquely guards thought.
Compare with nahw and balagha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: ولعلك علمت مما تلونا عليك في بيان الحاجة حد المنطق وتعريفه من قوانين تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر.
أنه علم. فصل موضوع كل علم ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه الذاتية له، كبدن الإنسان للطب، والكلمة والكلام لعلم النحو، فموضوع المنطق: المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لكن لا مطلقا بل من حيث إنها موصلة إلى المجهول التصوري والتصديقي. فائدة : اعلم أن لكل علم وصناعة غاية وإلا لكان طلبه عبثا والجد فيه لغوا.
تعصم مراعاتها إنما قال: "تعصم مراعاتها الذهن" لأن المنطق ليس نفسه يعصم الذهن عن الخطأ وإلا لم يعرض للمنطقي خطأ أصلا، وليس كذلك، فإنه ربما يخطئ لإهمال الآلة، فعلم أن العاصم مراعاة المنطق لا نفس المنطق.
في الفكر : هذا القيد يخرج العلوم القانونية التي لا تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الضلال في الفكر بل في المقال، كالعلوم العربية مثل النحو والمعاني والبيان.
عوارضه الذاتية: اعلم أن ما يعرض الشيء إما أن يكون عروضه لذاته أو لجزئه أو لأمر خارج عنه، والأمر الخارج عن المعروض إما مساو له أو أعم منه أو أخص منه أو مبائن له فالثلاثة الأول تسمى أعراضا ذاتية، والثلاثة الأخيرة، أي العارض لأمر خارج أعم من المعروض والعارض للخارج الأخص والعارض بسبب المبائن تسمى أعراضا غريبة، والعلوم يبحث فيها عن الأعراض الذاتية للموضوع من حيث: اعلم أن للحيثية ثلاثة أقسام الأولى هي الحيثية الإطلاقية، وهي لا تغير ذات المحيث ولا أحكامها والثانية هي الحيثية التقييدية، وهي تغير ذات المحيث وأحكامها. والثالثة: هي الحيثية التعليلية، وهي تغير الأحكام للمحيث دون ذاته. فإن شئت التفصيل فانظر في الحواشي المعلقة على "الحاشية الزاهدية".
وصناعة يحتمل أن يكون عطف الصناعة على العلم تفسيريا؛ إذ إطلاق الصناعة بمعنى العلم متعارف فيما بينهم. يقال: صناعة الميزان وصناعة البرهان ويحتمل أن يراد بالعلم ما لا يتعلق بكيفية عمل بل يكون المقصود منه نفس العلم، وبالصناعة ما يتعلق بكيفية عمل ويكون المقصود منه ذلك العمل.
غاية: أي مغائرة له خارجة عنه، والغاية متقدمة في التصور على تحصيل ذي الغاية؛ لأن تحصيله فعل اختياري، فلا بد أن يكون مسبوقا بتصور الغاية، فمن حق كل طالب علم أن يعلم الغاية المترتبة عليه المقصودة منه وأن يصدق ترتبها عليه وإلا لكان طلبه عبثا بلا فائدة والجدّ فيه لغوا بلا عائدة. ولما كان غاية علم الميزان الإصابة في الفكر
Chapter: Perhaps you have understood from what we recited to you in the explanation of the need the definition of logic: that it is laws whose observance guards the mind from error in thought.
That it is a science. Chapter: The subject of every science is that in which its essential accidents are investigated, like the human body for medicine, and the word and speech for the science of grammar. So the subject of logic is: conceptual and assertive information, but not absolutely, rather insofar as they lead to the unknown conceptual or assertive.
Benefit: Know that every science and art has a goal, otherwise seeking it would be futile and engaging in it meaningless.
"Whose observance guards" He said "whose observance guards the mind" because logic itself does not guard the mind from error; otherwise no logician would ever err, but that is not the case, for he may err due to neglect of the instrument. Thus it is known that the guardian is the observance of logic, not logic itself.
"In thought": this qualification excludes the normative sciences whose observance does not guard the mind from error in thought but in speech, like the Arabic sciences such as grammar, rhetoric, and clarity of speech.
"Its essential accidents": Know that what occurs to a thing either occurs to it by itself, or to its part, or to something external to it. The external thing is either equal to it, more general, more specific, or completely separate. The first three are called essential accidents, and the last three (i.e., the accident for an external thing more general than the subject, the accident for the external specific, and the accident due to the separate) are called accidental accidents. Sciences investigate the essential accidents of their subject. Know that the aspect has three divisions: first is the absolute aspect, which does not change the essence of the thing nor its rulings; second is the restrictive aspect, which changes the essence of the thing and its rulings; third is the causal aspect, which changes the rulings of the thing without its essence. If you want details, see the glosses on 'al-Hashiya al-Zahidiya'.
"And art" may be that 'art' is an explanatory conjunction to 'science'; for using 'art' in the sense of 'science' is customary among them. They say 'the art of the balance' and 'the art of demonstration'. Or it may be that by 'science' is meant what does not relate to the manner of action, but the purpose is the knowledge itself, and by 'art' what relates to the manner of action and the purpose is that action.
"Goal": meaning something other than it, external to it. The goal is prior in conception to the attainment of the thing with a goal; because its attainment is a voluntary act, so it must be preceded by conception of the goal. It is thus incumbent upon every seeker of knowledge to know the goal resulting from it and intended from it, and to affirm that it results from it; otherwise his seeking would be futile without benefit and his effort meaningless without return. Since the goal of the science of the balance is correctness in thought...
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage defines logic and elaborates on its subject and key concepts. It begins by recalling the definition of logic as 'laws whose observance guards the mind from error in thought'. The author then clarifies that logic is a science (ʿilm), and specifies its subject: conceptual and assertive information (al-maʿlūmāt al-taṣawwuriyya wa al-taṣdīqiyya), but not absolutely; only insofar as they lead to the unknown. The next point explains why the definition says 'whose observance guards' rather than 'which guards': logic itself does not guard, because logicians can err; rather, it is the observance (murāʿāt) of logical laws that prevents error. The phrase 'in thought' distinguishes logic from linguistic sciences like grammar, which prevent error in speech (maqāl) but not in thought. The author then discusses essential accidents (al-aʿrāḍ al-dhātiyya): what occurs to a thing either by itself, to its part, or externally. If external, it may be equal, more general, more specific, or separate. The first three are essential (dhātiyya), the last three are accidental (gharība). Sciences investigate essential accidents. Then the three aspects (al-haythiyyāt) are introduced: absolute (iṭlāqiyya – unchanged essence and rulings), restrictive (taqyīdiyya – changes both), and causal (taʿlīliyya – changes rulings only). The author also explains the terms 'science' and 'art': 'art' may be synonymous with 'science' or distinguished by practical application. Finally, the goal (ghāya) is discussed: every science has a goal conceptually prior to its pursuit, otherwise seeking it would be futile. The goal of logic is correctness in thought. The student should grasp the precise definition of logic, its subject, and the technical distinctions of accidents and aspects.

Key Terms
المنطق
مراعاة
الفكر
المقال

C) Because the definition applies only to Aristotelian logic.

Key Takeaway
Logic is a tool that guards the mind only through active observance, not by mere possession.

The Subject Matter of Logic and Essential Accidents
Defines the subject of logic and classifies essential accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of logic and classify accidents as essential or accidental according to the three categories.

Core Idea
Logic's subject is the known (information) insofar as it leads to the unknown, not absolutely.

This lesson examines the subject (mawḍūʿ) of logic, which is conceptual and assertive knowledge, but with a crucial qualifier: only in its function of leading to unknown knowledge. The passage also introduces the classification of essential vs. accidental accidents, a fundamental concept for any science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of logic
Find the sentence: 'فموضوع المنطق: المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لكن لا مطلقا بل من حيث إنها موصلة إلى المجهول التصوري والتصديقي.' The subject is not all information, but only that which helps discover unknown truths.
This distinguishes logic from other sciences that study the same information under different aspects.
2
Understand the qualifier
The phrase 'من حيث إنها موصلة' (insofar as it leads) is essential. Logic is not about any knowledge, but about knowledge as a means to further knowledge.
3
Classify essential vs. accidental accidents
Learn the three categories of essential accidents: those occurring to a thing by itself (li-dhātihi), by its part (li-juz'ihi), or by something external that is equal, more general, or more specific. The opposite three are accidental.
The text says: 'فالثلاثة الأول تسمى أعراضا ذاتية، والثلاثة الأخيرة ... تسمى أعراضا غريبة.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: ولعلك علمت مما تلونا عليك في بيان الحاجة حد المنطق وتعريفه من قوانين تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر.
أنه علم. فصل موضوع كل علم ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه الذاتية له، كبدن الإنسان للطب، والكلمة والكلام لعلم النحو، فموضوع المنطق: المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لكن لا مطلقا بل من حيث إنها موصلة إلى المجهول التصوري والتصديقي. فائدة : اعلم أن لكل علم وصناعة غاية وإلا لكان طلبه عبثا والجد فيه لغوا.
تعصم مراعاتها إنما قال: "تعصم مراعاتها الذهن" لأن المنطق ليس نفسه يعصم الذهن عن الخطأ وإلا لم يعرض للمنطقي خطأ أصلا، وليس كذلك، فإنه ربما يخطئ لإهمال الآلة، فعلم أن العاصم مراعاة المنطق لا نفس المنطق.
في الفكر : هذا القيد يخرج العلوم القانونية التي لا تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الضلال في الفكر بل في المقال، كالعلوم العربية مثل النحو والمعاني والبيان.
عوارضه الذاتية: اعلم أن ما يعرض الشيء إما أن يكون عروضه لذاته أو لجزئه أو لأمر خارج عنه، والأمر الخارج عن المعروض إما مساو له أو أعم منه أو أخص منه أو مبائن له فالثلاثة الأول تسمى أعراضا ذاتية، والثلاثة الأخيرة، أي العارض لأمر خارج أعم من المعروض والعارض للخارج الأخص والعارض بسبب المبائن تسمى أعراضا غريبة، والعلوم يبحث فيها عن الأعراض الذاتية للموضوع من حيث: اعلم أن للحيثية ثلاثة أقسام الأولى هي الحيثية الإطلاقية، وهي لا تغير ذات المحيث ولا أحكامها والثانية هي الحيثية التقييدية، وهي تغير ذات المحيث وأحكامها. والثالثة: هي الحيثية التعليلية، وهي تغير الأحكام للمحيث دون ذاته. فإن شئت التفصيل فانظر في الحواشي المعلقة على "الحاشية الزاهدية".
وصناعة يحتمل أن يكون عطف الصناعة على العلم تفسيريا؛ إذ إطلاق الصناعة بمعنى العلم متعارف فيما بينهم. يقال: صناعة الميزان وصناعة البرهان ويحتمل أن يراد بالعلم ما لا يتعلق بكيفية عمل بل يكون المقصود منه نفس العلم، وبالصناعة ما يتعلق بكيفية عمل ويكون المقصود منه ذلك العمل.
غاية: أي مغائرة له خارجة عنه، والغاية متقدمة في التصور على تحصيل ذي الغاية؛ لأن تحصيله فعل اختياري، فلا بد أن يكون مسبوقا بتصور الغاية، فمن حق كل طالب علم أن يعلم الغاية المترتبة عليه المقصودة منه وأن يصدق ترتبها عليه وإلا لكان طلبه عبثا بلا فائدة والجدّ فيه لغوا بلا عائدة. ولما كان غاية علم الميزان الإصابة في الفكر
Chapter: Perhaps you have understood from what we recited to you in the explanation of the need the definition of logic: that it is laws whose observance guards the mind from error in thought.
That it is a science. Chapter: The subject of every science is that in which its essential accidents are investigated, like the human body for medicine, and the word and speech for the science of grammar. So the subject of logic is: conceptual and assertive information, but not absolutely, rather insofar as they lead to the unknown conceptual or assertive.
Benefit: Know that every science and art has a goal, otherwise seeking it would be futile and engaging in it meaningless.
"Whose observance guards" He said "whose observance guards the mind" because logic itself does not guard the mind from error; otherwise no logician would ever err, but that is not the case, for he may err due to neglect of the instrument. Thus it is known that the guardian is the observance of logic, not logic itself.
"In thought": this qualification excludes the normative sciences whose observance does not guard the mind from error in thought but in speech, like the Arabic sciences such as grammar, rhetoric, and clarity of speech.
"Its essential accidents": Know that what occurs to a thing either occurs to it by itself, or to its part, or to something external to it. The external thing is either equal to it, more general, more specific, or completely separate. The first three are called essential accidents, and the last three (i.e., the accident for an external thing more general than the subject, the accident for the external specific, and the accident due to the separate) are called accidental accidents. Sciences investigate the essential accidents of their subject. Know that the aspect has three divisions: first is the absolute aspect, which does not change the essence of the thing nor its rulings; second is the restrictive aspect, which changes the essence of the thing and its rulings; third is the causal aspect, which changes the rulings of the thing without its essence. If you want details, see the glosses on 'al-Hashiya al-Zahidiya'.
"And art" may be that 'art' is an explanatory conjunction to 'science'; for using 'art' in the sense of 'science' is customary among them. They say 'the art of the balance' and 'the art of demonstration'. Or it may be that by 'science' is meant what does not relate to the manner of action, but the purpose is the knowledge itself, and by 'art' what relates to the manner of action and the purpose is that action.
"Goal": meaning something other than it, external to it. The goal is prior in conception to the attainment of the thing with a goal; because its attainment is a voluntary act, so it must be preceded by conception of the goal. It is thus incumbent upon every seeker of knowledge to know the goal resulting from it and intended from it, and to affirm that it results from it; otherwise his seeking would be futile without benefit and his effort meaningless without return. Since the goal of the science of the balance is correctness in thought...
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage defines logic and elaborates on its subject and key concepts. It begins by recalling the definition of logic as 'laws whose observance guards the mind from error in thought'. The author then clarifies that logic is a science (ʿilm), and specifies its subject: conceptual and assertive information (al-maʿlūmāt al-taṣawwuriyya wa al-taṣdīqiyya), but not absolutely; only insofar as they lead to the unknown. The next point explains why the definition says 'whose observance guards' rather than 'which guards': logic itself does not guard, because logicians can err; rather, it is the observance (murāʿāt) of logical laws that prevents error. The phrase 'in thought' distinguishes logic from linguistic sciences like grammar, which prevent error in speech (maqāl) but not in thought. The author then discusses essential accidents (al-aʿrāḍ al-dhātiyya): what occurs to a thing either by itself, to its part, or externally. If external, it may be equal, more general, more specific, or separate. The first three are essential (dhātiyya), the last three are accidental (gharība). Sciences investigate essential accidents. Then the three aspects (al-haythiyyāt) are introduced: absolute (iṭlāqiyya – unchanged essence and rulings), restrictive (taqyīdiyya – changes both), and causal (taʿlīliyya – changes rulings only). The author also explains the terms 'science' and 'art': 'art' may be synonymous with 'science' or distinguished by practical application. Finally, the goal (ghāya) is discussed: every science has a goal conceptually prior to its pursuit, otherwise seeking it would be futile. The goal of logic is correctness in thought. The student should grasp the precise definition of logic, its subject, and the technical distinctions of accidents and aspects.

Key Terms
الموضوع
المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية
العارض الذاتي
العارض الغريب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is an example of an essential accident (عرض ذاتي)?
A) The color of a book's cover
B) The ability to reason (nutq) for humans
C) The location of a mosque
D) The price of a horse

Key Takeaway
Logic's subject is the known only as a means to the unknown, and sciences investigate only essential accidents of their subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the subject of 'Tafsir' (Qur'anic exegesis). List three essential accidents that a mufassir would investigate (e.g., context of revelation (asbāb al-nuzūl), grammatical analysis (iʿrāb), and abrogation (naskh)). Explain why these are essential by the text's criteria.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
The Three Aspects (Haythiyyat) and the Goal of Logic
Explains the three aspects and the goal of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three aspects (haythiyyat) of a thing and apply them to examples from Islamic sciences, and also state the goal (ghāya) of logic.

Core Idea
The three aspects show how a thing can be considered absolutely, restrictively, or causally.

This lesson explains the three types of aspects (haythiyyāt) under which a thing can be considered: absolute, restrictive, and causal. These distinctions are crucial for precise reasoning. The lesson also covers the goal (ghāya) of logic and why it must be known before studying any science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of aspect
Find the sentence: 'للحيثية ثلاثة أقسام...' The three are: absolute (iṭlāqiyya), restrictive (taqyīdiyya), and causal (taʿlīliyya).
Each changes essence and/or rulings differently.
2
Understand the absolute aspect
The absolute aspect does not change the essence or rulings. Example: considering 'human' just as 'human' without any qualification.
3
Understand the restrictive aspect
The restrictive aspect changes both essence and rulings. Example: 'rational animal' (adds the differentia 'rational' to 'animal', changing the essence).
4
Understand the causal aspect
The causal aspect changes rulings but not essence. Example: considering a human as 'created' does not change the essence of humanity but changes rulings (e.g., obligation to worship).
5
Identify the goal of logic
The text states: 'وغاية علم الميزان الإصابة في الفكر' (the goal of the science of the balance is correctness in thought). This goal must be known in advance to avoid futility.

According to the text, which aspect changes the rulings of a thing without changing its essence?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the concept of 'prayer' (salāh). Consider it under the absolute aspect (just the act), the restrictive aspect (obligatory vs. supererogatory), and the causal aspect (the state that necessitates it, like time). Explain how each aspect affects rulings.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
5 min
Definition of Logic: Laws That Safeguard the Mind
Author defines logic and clarifies its protective role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of the definition of logic and explain why observance, not logic itself, safeguards the mind.

Core Idea
Logic is a tool whose use protects thought, not the tool itself.

In this passage, the author defines logic as a set of laws. He carefully clarifies that it is the observance of these laws that prevents error, not the mere possession of them. Let's unpack this definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence that defines logic. It starts with 'laws whose observance...'.
2
Identify the genus
The genus is 'laws' (qawaneen). Logic is a type of law or rule.
3
Identify the differentia
What distinguishes these laws? They safeguard the mind from error in thinking.
4
Understand the qualification
Note that it is the 'observance' (mura'at) of the laws that safeguards, not the laws themselves. The author gives the example that a logician can still err if he neglects the rules.
This is a crucial nuance: the tool is not automatic.
5
Apply to a familiar science
Consider how in fiqh, knowing the rules of usul al-fiqh does not automatically make one a correct mujtahid; one must actively apply them.

المحيث ولا أحكامها والثانية هي الحيثية التقييدية، وهى تغير ذات المحيث وأحكامها. والثالثة: هي الحيثية التعليلية، وهي تغير الأحكام للمحيث دون ذاته. فإن شئت التفصيل فانظر في الحواشي المعلقة على "الحاشية الزاهدية". وصناعة يحتمل أن يكون عطف الصناعة على العلم تفسيريا؛ إذ إطلاق الصناعة بمعنى العلم متعارف فيما بينهم. يقال: صناعة الميزان وصناعة البرهان ويحتمل أن يراد بالعلم ما لا يتعلق بكيفية عمل بل يكون المقصود منه نفس العلم، وبالصناعة ما يتعلق بكيفية عمل ويكون المقصود منه ذلك العمل. غاية: أي مغائرة له خارجة عنه، والغاية متقدمة في التصور على تحصيل ذي الغاية؛ لأن تحصيله فعل اختياري، فلا بد أن يكون مسبوقا بتصور الغاية، فمن حق كل طالب علم أن يعلم الغاية المترتبة عليه المقصودة منه وأن يصدق ترتبها عليه وإلا لكان طلبه عبثا بلا فائدة والجدّ فيه لغوا بلا عائدة. ولما كان غاية علم الميزان الإصابة في الفكر وحفظ الرأي عن الخطأ في النظر.
Chapter: Perhaps you learned from what we recited to you in the explanation of the need for logic its definition: 'laws whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thinking.' That it is a science. Chapter: The subject matter of every science is that in which its essential accidents are investigated, like the human body for medicine, and the word and speech for grammar. So the subject matter of logic is the conceptual and assential pieces of knowledge, but not absolutely, rather insofar as they lead to the unknown conceptual and assential. Benefit: Know that every science and craft has a purpose, otherwise seeking it would be futile and engaging in it idle. 'Whose observance safeguards' – he said 'whose observance safeguards the mind' because logic itself does not safeguard the mind from error; otherwise no logician would ever err, but that is not so, for he might err due to neglecting the instrument. So it is known that the safeguard is the observance of logic, not logic itself. 'In thinking' – this restriction excludes the normative sciences whose observance does not safeguard the mind from error in thinking but rather in speech, like the Arabic sciences such as grammar, rhetoric, and elucidation. 'Essential accidents': Know that what occurs to a thing either occurs to it by virtue of itself, or its part, or something external to it. The external thing relative to the subject is either equal to it, broader, narrower, or disparate. The first three are called essential accidents; the last three – i.e., what occurs due to something broader, or narrower, or disparate – are called accidental accidents. The sciences investigate essential accidents of the subject with respect to: Know that 'consideration' has three divisions: first is the absolute consideration, which does not change the essence of the thing considered nor its rulings; second is the restrictive consideration, which changes the essence and rulings; third is the explanatory consideration, which changes the rulings without the essence. If you wish detail, look in the glosses on 'al-Hashiyah al-Zahidiyyah'. 'Craft' – it is possible that the conjunction of 'craft' to 'science' is explicative, for using 'craft' in the sense of 'science' is conventional among them. They say 'the craft of logic' and 'the craft of demonstration'. It is also possible that by 'science' is meant that which does not pertain to the manner of action but whose intention is knowledge itself, and by 'craft' that which pertains to the manner of action whose intention is that action. 'Purpose': i.e., something distinct from it and external to it. The purpose is prior in conception to the attainment of that which has the purpose, because attaining it is a volitional act; it must be preceded by conception of the purpose. So every student of a science must know the purpose resulting from it intended from it, and affirm that it results from it; otherwise seeking it would be futile without benefit and engaging in it idle without advantage. And since the purpose of the science of logic is correctness in thinking and protecting opinion from error in reasoning.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage defines logic and clarifies its subject matter and purpose. The author begins by recalling the earlier discussion on the need for logic and gives its definition as 'laws whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thinking.' He then explains that logic is a science ('ilm), and each science has a subject matter (mawdu') in which it investigates essential accidents ('awarid dhatiyyah). For logic, the subject matter is conceptual and assential knowledge (al-ma'lumat al-tasawwuriyyah wa al-tasdiqiyyah), but only insofar as they lead to unknown conceptual and assential knowledge. The author then provides a benefit (fa'idah): every science and craft must have a purpose (ghayah), otherwise it would be futile. He elaborates on the wording 'whose observance safeguards the mind' to emphasize that logic itself does not automatically safeguard; rather, it is the observance of its rules that does. This is evidenced by the fact that logicians can err when they neglect the instrument. The phrase 'in thinking' (fi al-fikr) excludes normative sciences like grammar and rhetoric, which guard against error in speech, not thought. The author then explains essential accidents: what occurs to a thing either due to itself, its part, or something external; the first three (by itself, by its part, by something equal or broader or narrower) are essential, while those caused by what is broader, narrower, or disparate are accidental. Sciences investigate essential accidents only. He mentions three types of considerations (haythiyyat): absolute (not changing essence or rulings), restrictive (changing both), and explanatory (changing rulings only). The term 'craft' (sina'ah) is discussed as possibly synonymous with 'science' or as referring to practical knowledge. Finally, the author stresses the importance of knowing the purpose of a science: since it is volitional, one must conceive the purpose beforehand, and the purpose of logic is correctness in thinking and protecting opinion from error.

Key Terms
منطق
قوانين
مراعاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can a logician still commit an error?
A) Because logic is not a real science
B) Because he neglected to observe the laws of logic
C) Because the definition is incomplete
D) Because the subject matter is too narrow

Key Takeaway
Logic is a set of laws; it is the active observance of these laws that protects the mind from error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a definition of another Islamic science (e.g., 'ilm al-fiqh) using a similar structure: genus and differentia. Ensure the differentia specifies what it safeguards or aims at.
?
Identify a real case where a person knowing logic still made an error because they neglected to apply its rules. Use an example from Islamic scholarly debates.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner

Subject Matter of Logic: Knowledge That Leads to the Unknown
Author defines the subject matter of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of logic and explain why it is restricted to pieces of knowledge that lead to the unknown.

Core Idea
Logic studies not all knowledge, but knowledge as a tool for acquiring more knowledge.

The author explains that every science has a subject matter, and logic's subject matter is conceptual and assential knowledge—but only insofar as it leads to unknown knowledge. Let's break down this definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject matter of logic
The author says the subject is 'al-ma'lumat al-tasawwuriyyah wa al-tasdiqiyyah' (conceptual and assential knowledge).
2
Note the restriction
Find the phrase 'but not absolutely, rather insofar as they lead to the unknown.' Why is this restriction necessary?
Logic is concerned with knowledge that functions as a premise or definition.
3
Compare with other sciences
The author compares: medicine studies the human body, grammar studies words and speech. Each science has its own subject.
4
Understand the 'unknown'
The 'unknown' (majhul) is what we seek to know through reasoning. Logic studies how to move from known to unknown.
5
Apply to an Islamic science
Consider tafsir: the known is the Quranic text, the unknown is the interpretation. Logic helps in systematically deducing meanings.

Essential Accidents and the Purpose of Logic
Author explains essential accidents and the necessity of a purpose for every science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential accidents from accidental ones and explain why every science must have a purpose, specifically correctness in thinking for logic.

Core Idea
A science is defined by its essential accidents and driven by a purpose; logic's purpose is correctness in thinking.

The author delves into the theory of essential accidents and stresses that every science must have a purpose. He then applies these to logic. Let's understand these foundational ideas that structure all Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand essential vs. accidental
The author defines essential accidents: those that arise from the thing itself, its part, or something equal/broader/narrower. Accidental ones come from something disparate.
This is a key metaphysical distinction.
2
Identify the purpose of a science
The author says every science must have a purpose (ghayah), otherwise seeking it is futile.
3
Find logic's purpose
The purpose of logic is 'correctness in thinking and protecting opinion from error' (al-isabah fi al-fikr wa hifz al-ra'y 'an al-khata').
4
Connect purpose to subject
Why does logic need a purpose? Because it is a volitional act; one must conceive the end before engaging.
5
Apply to an Islamic science
For example, the purpose of 'ilm al-hadith is to preserve and authenticate the sayings of the Prophet. Its essential accidents include chain continuity, narrator reliability, etc.

The author then explains essential accidents: what occurs to a thing either due to itself, its part, or something external; the first three (by itself, by its part, by something equal or broader or narrower) are essential, while those caused by what is broader, narrower, or disparate are accidental. Sciences investigate essential accidents only. He mentions three types of considerations (haythiyyat): absolute (not changing essence or rulings), restrictive (changing both), and explanatory (changing rulings only). The term 'craft' (sina'ah) is discussed as possibly synonymous with 'science' or as referring to practical knowledge. Finally, the author stresses the importance of knowing the purpose of a science: since it is volitional, one must conceive the purpose beforehand, and the purpose of logic is correctness in thinking and protecting opinion from error.

Key Terms
عوارض ذاتية
عوارض غريبة
غاية
حيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of logic according to this passage?
A) To study words and speech
B) To protect the body from illness
C) Correctness in thinking and protecting opinion from error
D) To derive legal rulings

Key Takeaway
Every science studies essential accidents and must have a known purpose; logic's purpose is to achieve correctness in thought.

?

State the purpose of another Islamic science (e.g., tafsir) and explain why it must have a purpose, referring to the author's reasoning about volitional acts.

beginner
5 min
Definition of Signification and Its Divisions
Author defines signification and divides it into linguistic and non-linguistic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define signification (dalalah) and distinguish its two main divisions: linguistic and non-linguistic.

Core Idea
Signification is the key that unlocks the relationship between words and meanings in logic.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of signification (dalalah), which is the starting point of logic. Understanding how signs convey meanings is essential for studying definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the technical definition
Find the precise definition of signification: 'the state of a thing such that knowledge of it entails knowledge of something else.' Understand the two components: the signifier (dal) and the signified (madlul).
The definition is presented both linguistically and technically.
2
Recognize the two divisions
Note that signification is divided into linguistic (lafdhiyyah) and non-linguistic (ghayr lafdhiyyah). Linguistic signification uses words; non-linguistic uses other signs.
Each division is further subdivided into three types, as mentioned by the author.
3
Understand the logician's interest in words
Although logic is about meanings, the logician must study words because giving and receiving benefit depends on them. Words are the vehicle for conveying arguments and definitions.
This justifies why logic books discuss signification and language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الدلالة الدلالة لغة هو الإرشاد أي راه نمودن وفي الاصطلاح كون الشيء بحيث يلزم من العلم به العلم بشيء آخر. والدلالة قسمان لفظية وغير لفظية، واللفظية: = - وحفظ الرأي عن الخطأ في النظر ، فمن أراد الشروع فيه على وجه البصيرة فلا بد من أن يعلم أنه علم بقوانين تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر؛ فإن من علمه بهذا الوجه فإنه يعلم غايته ويصدق ترتبها عليه. لا شغل فيه أربع لغات بضم الشين وسكون الغين المعجمة، أو ضمهما، وفتح الشين وسكون الغين، أو فتحهما من حيث إنما قيّد بالحيثية؛ لأن المنطقي إذا كان نحويا أيضا فله شغل بالألفاظ، لكن لا من حيث إنه منطقي بل من حيث إنه نحوي كذا أفاد السيد المحقق. بمعزل إلخ إذ لا غرض للمنطقي إلا عن القول الشارح والحجة وكيفية ترتيبهما حتى يوصلا إلى مجهول تصوري أو تصديقي، وليس الموصل ألفاظهما بل معانيهما. الإفادة إلخ : اعلم أنه لما كان الإنسان مدني الطبع لا يمكن تعيشه إلا بمشاركة من أبناء نوعه افتقر كل واحد في مأكله ومشربه وملبسه إلى أن يظهر ما في ضميره، فأفادهم واستفاد منهم، وأعان على مقاصدهم ومصالحهم، فلما كان يؤدي هذا الغرض يسمّى دالا، والمؤدى مدلولا. والدال إن كان لفظا فالدلالة لفظية وإلا فغير لفظية وكل منهما على ثلاثة أقسام كما قال المصنف مله كون الشيء إلخ: فإن قلت: إن الدلالة تتوقف على علم المدلول وهو يتوقف على الدلالة، وهذا دور؟ قلت: إن علم المدلول من الدال موقوف على الدلالة لا علم المدلول مطلقا، والدلالة تتوقف على علم المدلول مطلقا لا على المدلول من الدال فلا دور. يلزم إلخ: اعلم أن المراد باللزوم ههنا أعم من أن يكون عقليا أو عرفيا والأول أي: اللزوم العقلي هو ما يمتنع تصور الملزوم بدون اللازم عقلا كلزوم الزوجية للأربعة، فإن العقل إذا تصور معنى الأربعة يمتنع عدم تصور معنى الزوجية والثاني :أي: اللزوم العرفي هو ما يمتنع تصور الملزوم بدون اللازم عرفا وإن لم يمتنع عقلا، كلزوم الجود للحاتم فإن العقل يجوز أن يتصور الحاتم بدون الجود لكن لكثرة صدور الجود عن الحاتم صار كأنه لازم له.
Chapter on Signification: Signification linguistically is guidance; in technical terminology, it is the state of a thing such that knowledge of it entails knowledge of something else. Signification is of two types: linguistic and non-linguistic. Linguistic signification: = - And protecting opinion from error in reasoning, so whoever wishes to begin it with insight must know that it is a science of laws whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thinking; for whoever knows it in this way knows its purpose and affirms its result. There is no concern for the logician with words from the perspective of being a logician. How? This investigation is separate from his aim and purpose; nonetheless, he must investigate words signifying meanings, because giving and receiving benefit depends on it. Therefore the investigation of signification and words is presented in logic books. 'No concern' has four readings: with damm on the shin and sukun on the ghayn, or damm on both, or fatha on the shin and sukun on the ghayn, or fatha on both. He restricted it with 'from the perspective' because if the logician is also a grammarian, he has concern with words, but not as a logician rather as a grammarian. Thus the master investigator explained. 'Separate' etc.: For the logician has no aim except the defining statement and the argument and how to arrange them so that they lead to an unknown concept or assertion, and what leads is not their words but their meanings. 'Giving and receiving benefit' etc.: Know that since humans are social by nature, their livelihood is only possible through participation with their fellow humans; so each one needs to reveal what is in his mind regarding food, drink, and clothing, so he benefits them and receives benefit from them, aiding their aims and interests. Since it serves this purpose, it is called a signifier, and the thing conveyed is the signified. If the signifier is a word, the signification is linguistic; otherwise it is non-linguistic. Each of them is divided into three types, as the author said. 'The state of a thing such that' etc.: If you say: Signification depends on knowledge of the signified, and knowledge of the signified depends on signification—this is a circle? The answer: Knowledge of the signified from the signifier depends on signification, not knowledge of the signified absolutely; and signification depends on knowledge of the signified absolutely, not on knowledge of the signified from that signifier; so there is no circle. 'Entails' etc.: Know that what is meant by entailment here is more general, whether rational or customary. The first, i.e., rational entailment, is where it is impossible to conceive the entailed without the entailer rationally, like the entailment of evenness for four, for the mind, when it conceives the meaning of four, it is impossible not to conceive the meaning of evenness. The second, i.e., customary entailment, is where it is impossible to conceive the entailed without the entailer customarily, even if not impossible rationally, like the entailment of generosity for Hatim, for the mind can conceive Hatim without generosity, but due to the frequent occurrence of generosity from Hatim, it became as if it were necessary.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage defines signification (dalalah) and its divisions. The author begins with the linguistic meaning: guidance (irshad). Technically, it is 'the state of a thing such that knowledge of it entails knowledge of something else.' He divides signification into linguistic (lafdhiyyah) and non-linguistic (ghayr lafdhiyyah). He then explains why a logician must study words: although his aim is meanings, giving and receiving information depends on words, hence the need to discuss signification. The author addresses an objection about circularity: if signification depends on prior knowledge of the signified, and knowledge of the signified depends on signification, this seems circular. The resolution is that knowledge of the signified from a particular signifier depends on signification, but signification depends on absolute knowledge of the signified (perhaps through other means), so no circularity. Finally, he explains entailment (luzum) as the relation in signification: it can be rational (aqli), where the connection is necessary by reason (e.g., four entails evenness), or customary (urfi), where the connection is common but not necessary (e.g., Hatim entails generosity). This distinction is important for understanding how words signify meanings—through conventional association.

Key Terms
دلالة
لفظية
غير لفظية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Guidance to something
B) The state of a thing such that knowledge of it entails knowledge of something else
C) The use of words to convey meanings
D) Any sign that indicates a meaning

Key Takeaway
Signification is the relation where knowledge of one thing leads to knowledge of another, and it is divided into linguistic and non-linguistic types.

7 min
Resolving the Circularity Objection
Author responds to a potential circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and resolve the alleged circularity in signification by distinguishing absolute knowledge from knowledge via a specific signifier.

Core Idea
The circle in signification is broken by distinguishing absolute knowledge from knowledge via a specific signifier.

Some critics argue that defining signification leads to a vicious circle: to know the signified, you need the signification, but to have signification, you need prior knowledge of the signified. The author provides a precise resolution that saves the definition from circularity.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objection is: 'Signification depends on knowledge of the signified, and knowledge of the signified depends on signification. This is a circle.'
This is a common critique against definitions of relational concepts.
2
Distinguish two types of knowledge
The author distinguishes 'knowledge of the signified from the signifier' (which depends on signification) from 'absolute knowledge of the signified' (which is independent of that specific signifier).
Absolute knowledge may come from other sources.
3
Show the dependence relations
Signification depends on absolute knowledge of the signified (we must know what is signified in general), but not on knowledge from that particular signifier. Conversely, knowledge of the signified from the signifier depends on signification. Thus the circle is broken.
The dependence is one-way in each direction, not mutual in the same sense.
4
Conclude no vicious circle
Since the two dependencies are on different types of knowledge, there is no circularity. The objection confuses absolute knowledge with knowledge from the signifier.
This resolution is standard in Islamic logic.

Rational and Customary Entailment
Author explains rational and customary entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish rational entailment (aqli) from customary entailment (urfi) in the context of signification.

Core Idea
Entailment can be based on reason or on convention, and both play a role in how signs convey meanings.

The relation between signifier and signified is called entailment (luzum). This lesson explores its two types: rational, where the connection is necessary by reason, and customary, where it is based on frequent association. Understanding this distinction helps clarify how words come to signify meanings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of luzum
Luzum is the entailment relation that holds between the signifier and the signified. It is broad enough to include both necessary and conventional connections.
The author emphasizes that luzum here is general.
2
Examine rational entailment
Rational entailment is where it is impossible to conceive the signifier without the signified by reason. Example: the concept of four entails evenness. If you understand 'four', you necessarily understand that it is even.
This is a logical necessity.
3
Examine customary entailment
Customary entailment is where the connection is so frequent that it becomes habitually associated, though not rationally necessary. Example: the name 'Hatim' entails generosity, even though one can conceive Hatim without being generous.
This is based on custom or convention.
4
Relate to linguistic signification
Linguistic signification is primarily a form of customary entailment: the connection between a word and its meaning is established by convention, not by rational necessity.
This is why different languages have different words for the same meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

There is no concern for the logician with words from the perspective of being a logician. How? This investigation is separate from his aim and purpose; nonetheless, he must investigate words signifying meanings, because giving and receiving benefit depends on it. Therefore the investigation of signification and words is presented in logic books. 'No concern' has four readings: with damm on the shin and sukun on the ghayn, or damm on both, or fatha on the shin and sukun on the ghayn, or fatha on both. He restricted it with 'from the perspective' because if the logician is also a grammarian, he has concern with words, but not as a logician rather as a grammarian. Thus the master investigator explained. 'Separate' etc.: For the logician has no aim except the defining statement and the argument and how to arrange them so that they lead to an unknown concept or assertion, and what leads is not their words but their meanings. 'Giving and receiving benefit' etc.: Know that since humans are social by nature, their livelihood is only possible through participation with their fellow humans; so each one needs to reveal what is in his mind regarding food, drink, and clothing, so he benefits them and receives benefit from them, aiding their aims and interests. Since it serves this purpose, it is called a signifier, and the thing conveyed is the signified. If the signifier is a word, the signification is linguistic; otherwise it is non-linguistic. Each of them is divided into three types, as the author said. 'The state of a thing such that' etc.: If you say: Signification depends on knowledge of the signified, and knowledge of the signified depends on signification—this is a circle? The answer: Knowledge of the signified from the signifier depends on signification, not knowledge of the signified absolutely; and signification depends on knowledge of the signified absolutely, not on knowledge of the signified from that signifier; so there is no circle. 'Entails' etc.: Know that what is meant by entailment here is more general, whether rational or customary. The first, i.e., rational entailment, is where it is impossible to conceive the entailed without the entailer rationally, like the entailment of evenness for four, for the mind, when it conceives the meaning of four, it is impossible not to conceive the meaning of evenness. The second, i.e., customary entailment, is where it is impossible to conceive the entailed without the entailer customarily, even if not impossible rationally, like the entailment of generosity for Hatim, for the mind can conceive Hatim without generosity, but due to the frequent occurrence of generosity from Hatim, it became as if it were necessary.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage defines signification (dalalah) and its divisions. The author begins with the linguistic meaning: guidance (irshad). Technically, it is 'the state of a thing such that knowledge of it entails knowledge of something else.' He divides signification into linguistic (lafdhiyyah) and non-linguistic (ghayr lafdhiyyah). He then explains why a logician must study words: although his aim is meanings, giving and receiving information depends on words, hence the need to discuss signification. The author addresses an objection about circularity: if signification depends on prior knowledge of the signified, and knowledge of the signified depends on signification, this seems circular. The resolution is that knowledge of the signified from a particular signifier depends on signification, but signification depends on absolute knowledge of the signified (perhaps through other means), so no circularity. Finally, he explains entailment (luzum) as the relation in signification: it can be rational (aqli), where the connection is necessary by reason (e.g., four entails evenness), or customary (urfi), where the connection is common but not necessary (e.g., Hatim entails generosity). This distinction is important for understanding how words signify meanings—through conventional association.

Key Terms
لزوم
عقلي
عرفي
زوجية
حاتم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is an example of rational entailment given in the passage?
A) Hatim entails generosity
B) Smoke entails fire
C) Four entails evenness
D) Word entails meaning

Key Takeaway
Entailment in signification is either rational, based on logical necessity, or customary, based on frequent association.

Classify the following entailments as rational or customary: a) The number 2 entails being even. b) The name 'Yusuf' entails patience (if Yusuf is known as a prophet of patience). c) The word 'water' entails wetness.

For each, explain why the entailment holds according to the definitions given.

Six Types of Signification and the Natural vs Rational Dispute
Enumeration of six signification types and scholarly dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six types of signification and explain the disagreement between al-Sayyid al-Sanad and al-Dawwani about natural signification.

Core Idea
The six types are inductive; al-Sayyid restricts natural to words, al-Dawwani includes non-verbal signs.

The author introduces a sixfold classification of signification, but immediately a tension arises: is natural signification confined to words or does it include natural signs like redness for shame? This dispute reveals the careful distinctions required in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six types from the passage
The passage says 'فهذه ست دلالات' (so these are six significations). From the subsequent discussion, we can infer the six: verbal (lafziyya), mental (dhihniyya), natural (tabi'iyya), rational ('aqliyya), gestural (ishariyya), and written (kitabiyya). Notice that the text mentions natural, rational, gestures, and writing specifically.
The sixth type (mental) is implied by the context; it refers to concepts in the mind.
2
Understand the dispute about natural signification
Al-Sayyid al-Sanad argues natural signification is only for words, denying non-verbal natural signs. Al-Dawwani says natural includes non-verbal signs like redness for shame. The commentator reconciles by distinguishing two aspects: the causal relation (rational) and the innate association (natural).
Thus the same phenomenon can be both natural and rational depending on perspective.
3
Evaluate the reconciliation
The commentator notes that if we deny the natural category because rational is present, we would also have to deny that verbal signification is natural, since it too involves a causal relation. This shows the classification is not exclusive but complementary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه ست دلالات والمنطقي إنما يبحث عن الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية؛ لأن الإفادة للغير والاستفادة من الغير إنما يتيسر بها بسهولة بخلاف غيرها؛ فإن الإفادة والاستفادة بها لا يخلو عن صعوبة، هذا. فهذه ست إلخ الاحتمالات الستة استقرائي لا عقلي وذهب السيد السند إلى أن الأقسام خمسة، وأنكر الطبعي الغير اللفظي حيث صرح في حاشيته على شرح المطالع : بأن الدلالة الطبعية هي الألفاظ فقط، والدلالة العقلية تعم اللفظ وغيره. وقال المحقق الدواني في حواشي التهذيب : الطبعية لا تنحصر في اللفظ؛ فإن دلالة الحمرة على الخجل والصفرة على الوجل منها. ولعل السيد - قدس سره - نظر إلى أن الدال في هذه الأمثلة أثر للمدلول، فيكون الدلالة فيها عقلية؛ لأن الدلالة بعلاقة التأثير عقلية كما قدمنا والتحقيق أن ههنا جهتين جهة التأثير، وجهة إحداث الطبعية. من جهة الأول عقلية ومن جهة الثاني طبعية، فغاية الأمر أن العقلية تحققت في مواد الطبعية وهو غير موجب لإنكارها وإلا لزم أن لا تكون اللفظية أيضا طبعية، فإنها أيضا لا تخلو عن علاقة التأثير، كما لا يخفى. بسهو :لة لأن الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية أسهل الدلالات تعليما وتعلما، وأما غيرها ففيه صعوبة، ولا يكتفي لإظهار ما في ضميره. أما الدلالة الطبعية وكذا العقلية فهي غير كافية للفهم المفصل، وأما الإشارات فأيضا للدلالة غير كافية، وفي الكتابة مشقة عظيمة، فاحتيج في التعلم والتعليم إلى الألفاظ الموضوعة بإزاء ما في ضمائرهم، واختص نظر المنطقي بالدلالة اللفظية الوضعية. هذا أي: خُذ هذا وله وجهان: الأول: أن يكون هذا مفعولا لفعل محذوف وهو "خُذ"، والثاني: أن يكون "خذ" و "ذا" اسم إشارة مفعوله. والثاني لا يساعده رسم الخط. "ها" اسم فعل بمعنى في المحاورات إلخ: اعلم أن الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية بجميع أقسامها - أعني المطابقة والتضمن والالتزام – معتبرة في المحاورات. وأما في العلوم فقيل: إن الالتزام مهجور؛ فإنه عقلي. والجمهور على أنه معتبر في العلوم أيضا. وههنا تحقيقات تطلب من مظانها.
So these are six significations, and the logician only investigates verbal conventional signification because communicating to others and learning from others is only facilitated by it easily, unlike other types; for communication and learning through them is not free of difficulty. This. 'So these are six etc.' The six probabilities are inductive, not rational. Al-Sayyid al-Sanad held that the divisions are five, and he denied the natural non-verbal, as he stated in his gloss on Sharh al-Matali': that natural signification is only for words, and rational signification encompasses words and other things. And al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani said in his glosses on al-Tahdhib: natural is not restricted to words; for signification of redness to shame and yellowness to fear is among it. Perhaps al-Sayyid—may his secret be sanctified—considered that the signifier in these examples is an effect of the signified, so the signification is rational; because signification by causal relation is rational as we presented. And the verification is that there are two aspects: the aspect of effect, and the aspect of producing naturalness. From the first aspect it is rational, from the second it is natural. So the upshot is that the rational is realized in the materials of the natural, and this does not necessitate denying it; otherwise it would follow that the verbal is also not natural, for it too is not devoid of a causal relation, as is not hidden. [Misspelled?] 'because verbal conventional signification is the easiest of significations in teaching and learning. As for other types, there is difficulty in them, and they are not sufficient to express what is in one's mind. As for natural and rational signification, they are insufficient for detailed understanding, and as for gestures, they are also insufficient for signification, and writing involves great hardship. So teaching and learning require words placed corresponding to what is in their hearts, and the logician's consideration is specific to verbal conventional signification. 'This' means: take this, and it has two aspects: first, that 'this' is an object of a deleted verb 'take'; second, that 'take' and 'this' are a demonstrative pronoun as its object. The second is not supported by orthography. 'Ha' is a verb meaning 'take'... 'in conversations etc.': Know that verbal conventional signification in all its divisions—namely mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam—is considered in conversations. As for the sciences, it is said that iltizam is abandoned because it is rational. And the majority hold that it is considered in the sciences as well. And here there are investigations to be sought from their sources.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that enumerates six types of signification and explains the logician's focus on verbal conventional (wad'iyya) signification. The author first states the matn: 'So these are six significations, and the logician only investigates verbal conventional signification because it is the easiest for teaching and learning; other types involve difficulty.' Then the commentary begins by noting that the enumeration of six possibilities is inductive (istighra'i) not rational ('aqli). It then presents a dispute: al-Sayyid al-Sanad argued that the divisions are actually five, denying the existence of natural non-verbal signification, claiming that natural signification (tabi'iyya) is only for words. In contrast, al-Dawwani held that natural signification is not restricted to words; for example, redness indicating shame and yellowness indicating fear are natural significations. The commentator reconciles these views by noting that al-Sayyid considered the signifier in these examples to be an effect of the signified, making it rational (since signification by causal relation is rational). However, the correct view is that two aspects exist: the aspect of causation (effect) is rational, and the aspect of innate association (tabi'iyya) is natural. The rational is realized in the materials of the natural, but that does not negate the natural as a distinct type; otherwise, even verbal signification would not be natural, since it too involves causal relations. The commentary then elaborates on the ease of verbal signification, contrasting it with natural, rational, gestural, and written forms, emphasizing that verbal conventional signification alone suffices for detailed understanding and is therefore the focus of logic. Finally, the commentator explains the word 'this' (hadha) in the matn, discussing its grammatical analysis, and notes that verbal conventional signification in all three divisions (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) is considered valid in ordinary discourse, with a disagreement about iltizam in the sciences: some consider it abandoned (because it is rational), but the majority accept it.

Key Terms
الدلالة
اللفظية الوضعية
الطبعية
العقلية

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the result of the dispute between al-Sayyid al-Sanad and al-Dawwani?
A) Al-Sayyid's view is rejected entirely.
B) Both are correct because natural and rational signification can coincide in the same instance but are conceptually distinct.
C) Al-Dawwani's view is preferred, and natural is only verbal.
D) The dispute is unresolved.

Key Takeaway
The six types are not rigidly separate; natural and rational signification often overlap, and the classification is inductive, not a priori.

Why Logic Focuses on Verbal Conventional Signification and Its Divisions
Rationale for prioritizing verbal conventional and its divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians prioritize verbal conventional signification and identify its three divisions (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) with examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Verbal conventional signification is the easiest and most precise for teaching, and it alone has the three divisions needed for detailed understanding.

After settling the classification, the author explains why logic concerns itself only with verbal conventional signification: it is easy, precise, and sufficient for expressing all meanings. Additionally, within this type, there are three important divisions that are crucial in both everyday conversation and scholarly discourse.

Guided Path
1
Find the reasons for focusing on verbal conventional
The text states: 'لأن الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية أسهل الدلالات تعليما وتعلما' (because verbal conventional signification is the easiest for teaching and learning). Other types like natural, rational, gestures, and writing are either insufficient for detailed understanding or involve great hardship.
2
Identify the three divisions mentioned
The passage then introduces the three divisions: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (implication by inclusion), and iltizam (implication by necessity). It says that in conversations all three are considered valid.
Mutabaqa is the direct meaning, tadammun refers to a part of the meaning, iltizam is an associated meaning.
3
Understand the disagreement about iltizam in sciences
A divergence is noted: some say iltizam is abandoned in sciences because it is rational (i.e., not purely conventional), but the majority hold that it is considered in sciences as well.
This shows that the scope of iltizam is debated among scholars.
