8 min
The Three Types of Verbal Conventional Signification
Author defines the three kinds of verbal conventional signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mutabiqiyya, tadammuniyya, and iltizamiyya and solve the famous problem of perspectival confusion in iltizamiyya.

Core Idea
A word signifies its whole meaning, a part, or an external necessary.

Understanding how words convey meaning is crucial for Islamic logic. The author defines three kinds of verbal conventional signification that are used in sciences and conversations. In this lesson, we unpack each type and address a common objection that arises when a word like 'shams' is used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the coined meaning
Determine the meaning for which the word was originally coined. For example, 'insan' was coined for 'animal and rational'.
2
Classify the intended indication
Is the intended indication the entire coined meaning (mutabiqiyya), a part of it (tadammuniyya), or an external meaning that is mentally necessitated by the coined meaning (iltizamiyya)?
If it's external, check if it is necessarily concomitant.
3
Apply the perspectival rule for iltizamiyya
When a word indicates a necessary external meaning, it must be from the perspective of that necessity, not from the perspective of indicating the whole meaning. This resolves the famous problem where 'shams' indicates light (necessary) and also the luminous body (whole), but the significations differ in perspective.
The author omitted this qualification, but the commentator clarifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: وينبغي أن يعلم أن الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية التي لها العبرة في المحاورات والعلوم على ثلاثة أنحاء: أحدها: المطابقية، وهي أن يدل اللفظ على تمام ما وضع له ذلك اللفظ له كدلالة الإنسان على مجموع الحيوان والناطق، وثانيها: التضمنية، وهي أن يدل اللفظ على جزء المعنى الموضوع له كدلالته على الحيوان فقط، وثالثها: الدلالة الالتزامية، وهي أن لا يدل اللفظ على الموضوع له ولا على جزئه، بل على معنى خارج لازم للموضوع له واللازم هو ما ينتقل الذهن من الموضوع له إليه. وذلك اللفظ له إلخ من حيث إنه وضع له. وإنما قيدناها بهذه الحيثية لدفع الإشكال المشهور: وهو أن اللفظ مثلا كالشمس إذا وضع للملزوم كالجرم النوري، واللازم كالضوء، وأريد اللازم - أي الضوء - من جهة أنه لازم لملزومه الموضوع له - أعني الجرم النوري - يكون الدلالة حينئذ التزامية مع أنه يصدق على هذه الدلالة أنها دلالة اللفظ على تمام ما وضع له فيكون هذه الدلالة مطابقية. ودفعه بأن الدلالة المذكورة وإن كانت دلالة اللفظ على تمام ما وضع له لكنها ليس من حيث إنه تمام ما وضع له بل من حيث إنه لازم لملزومه الموضوع له، فظهر أن ترك الحيثية من مسامحة الماتن. وثانيها إلخ : اعلم أن ههنا أي في دلالة التضمن مذهبين مذهب أهل الميزان، وهم لم يعتبروا في الدلالة القصد، بل الفهم فقط، فدلالة اللفظ الموضوع للمعنى المركب على الأجزاء المفهومة في ضمن المعنى المركب بحيث لم يتعلق القصد بها بالذات تضمنية. وأهل العربية اعتبروا القصد فلا تكون تضمنية عندهم. والحق مذهب أهل الميزان؛ فإن على مذهب أهل العربية يبطل الحصر، فإن الدلالة التضمنية الميزانية لا تدخل في شيء من الدلالات مع أنها داخلة في المقسم. أن لازم إنما اعتبر اللزوم في هذه الدلالة؛ لأنها دلالة اللفظ على ما خرج عن المعنى الموضوع له ولا خفاء في اللفظ لا يدل على كل أمر خارج عنه، فلا بد لدلالته على الخارج من شرط، وهو اللزوم الذهني، أي كون الأمر الخارج لازما لمسمّى اللفظ بحيث يلزم من تصور المسمّى تصوره فإنه لو لم يتحقق هذا الشرط لامتنع فهم الأمر الخارج من اللفظ فلم يكن دالا عليه. واللازم الخ: لما وقع في تعريف دلالة الالتزام ذكر اللازم ولم يعلم معناه قبل ذلك ذكر معناه وقال: "واللازم إلخ" اعلم أيها المتعلم الفطن أوّلا، اللازم على قسمين عرفي وعقلي. وقد بينا معناهما سابقا. أورد المصنف محليه مثالين: الأول للأول، والثاني للثاني. وثانيا أن اللازم أيضا على قسمين: أحدهما: اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم: وهو ما إذا تصور الملزوم واللازم يجزم باللزوم بينهما ضرورة وثانيهما: اللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص: وهو أن يلزم من تصور الملزوم تصور اللازم. وهذا المعنى هو المراد عندهم في تعريف الدلالة الالتزامية. فظهر بهذا أن تمثيل اللازم الواقع في دلالة الالتزام بقابل العلم وصنعة الكتابة لا يصح على مذاقهم؛ لظهور أنه لا يلزم من تصور الإنسان تصور قابل العلم وصنعة الكتابة، بل هما مثالان للازم البين بالمعنى الأعم، فإن من تصور =
Section: And it should be known that verbal conventional signification, which is considered in conversations and sciences, is of three kinds: First: mutabiqiyya (matching), which is that the word indicates the entire meaning for which it was coined, like the indication of 'insan' (human) for the combination of animal and rational. Second: tadammuniyya (implication), which is that the word indicates a part of the meaning for which it was coined, like its indication for 'animal' only. Third: iltizamiyya (entaliment), which is that the word does not indicate the coining-meaning nor its part, but rather an external meaning necessarily concomitant (lazim) to the coining-meaning, and the lāzim is what the mind transfers from the coining-meaning to it. 'That word for it etc.' from the perspective that it is coined for it. And we only qualified it with this perspective to repel the famous problem: that a word like 'shams' (sun) is coined for the entailed thing (al-malzum) like the luminous body, and the necessary (al-lazim) like light, and if the necessary—i.e., light—is intended from the aspect that it is necessary for its entailed thing—i.e., the luminous body—then the signification would be iltizamiyya, while it is also true that this signification is the word's indication of the entire coining-meaning, so it would be mutabiqiyya. The repulsion is that the said signification, although it is the word's indication of the entire coining-meaning, is not from the perspective of being the entire coining-meaning but from the perspective of being necessary for its entailed thing. So it appears that dropping the perspective is a looseness of the author. 'And its second etc.': Know that here—i.e., in tadammun signification—there are two schools: the school of ahl al-mizan (logicians), who did not consider intention in signification but only understanding. So the signification of a word coined for a composite meaning about the parts understood within the composite meaning such that the intention is not specifically attached to them is tadammuniyya. The school of ahl al-'arabiyya (grammarians) considered intention, so it is not tadammuniyya according to them. The truth is the school of ahl al-mizan; for according to the school of ahl al-'arabiyya, the enumeration is invalidated, because the tadammuniyya of the logicians does not enter into any of the divisions while it is included in the divided category. That: necessity (luzum) is only considered in this signification because it is the word's indication of what is outside the coining-meaning, and it is not hidden that the word does not indicate every external thing. So for its indication of the external, a condition is necessary, which is mental necessity (al-luzum al-dhihni), i.e., that the external thing is necessary for the meaning of the word such that the conceptualization of the meaning entails its conceptualization. For if this condition is not realized, understanding the external from the word would be impossible, so it would not be indicative of it. 'And the necessary etc.': Since in the definition of iltizamiyya signification the necessary (lazim) was mentioned and its meaning was not known before, he mentions its meaning and says: 'And the necessary etc.' Know, O intelligent student, first that the necessary is two kinds: customary ('urfi) and rational ('aqli). We have already explained their meanings. The author provided two examples for them: the first for the first, the second for the second. Second, the necessary is also two kinds: one: the necessary evident in the more general sense (al-lazim al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'amm): that when the entailed and the necessary are conceptualized, one judges the necessity between them necessarily. The second: the necessary evident in the more specific sense (al-lazim al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass): that conceptualizing the entailed necessitates conceptualizing the necessary. And this meaning is what is intended in their definition of iltizamiyya signification. So it appears from this that representing the necessary occurring in iltizamiyya signification by 'capable of knowledge' and 'craft of writing' is not correct according to their taste; because it is clear that conceptualizing a human does not necessitate conceptualizing 'capable of knowledge' and 'craft of writing'. Rather, both are examples of the necessary evident in the more general sense, for whoever conceptualizes =
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on the three kinds of verbal conventional signification: mutabiqiyya (matching), tadammuniyya (implication by part), and iltizamiyya (entaliment of a necessary concomitant). The author begins by stating the matn that verbal conventional signification used in conversations and sciences is of three types. First, mutabiqiyya: the word signifies the entire meaning for which it was coined, like 'insan' signifying the combination of animal and rational. Second, tadammuniyya: the word signifies a part of that meaning, like 'insan' signifying 'animal' only. Third, iltizamiyya: the word signifies not the coining-meaning nor its part, but an external meaning that is necessarily concomitant (lazim) to the coining-meaning, and the necessary is that to which the mind transfers from the coining-meaning. The commentary then addresses a famous problem: if a word like 'shams' is coined for the luminous body (malzum), and the necessary (light) is intended, the signification would be iltizamiyya, yet it also fits mutabiqiyya since the word indicates its entire coining-meaning? The resolution is that the perspectival qualification (al-haythiyya) must be considered: the signification is iltizamiyya not from the perspective of indicating the entire coining-meaning, but from the perspective of the necessary relation. The author's omission of this qualification in the definition is merely a looseness (musamaha). Next, the commentary discusses tadammuniyya, noting two schools: ahl al-mizan (logicians) who do not require intention (qasd) for signification—only understanding suffices—so a word coined for a composite meaning implies its parts regardless of intention. Ahl al-'arabiyya (grammarians) require intention, so for them such implication is not tadammuniyya. The commentator sides with the logicians, arguing that the grammarians' view invalidates the enumeration (hijr) because the logicians' tadammuniyya would not fit any of the divisions while being part of the divided category (maqsama). Then the commentary explains why necessity (luzum) is crucial for iltizamiyya: the word does not indicate every external thing, so a mental necessity is required—the external must be such that conceptualizing the coining-meaning entails conceptualizing the necessary. Without this, the word could not indicate it. Finally, the commentary defines the necessary (lazim) in two binary divisions: (1) customary vs. rational, with examples given; (2) evident in the more general sense (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'amm)—where one judges the necessity immediately upon conceptualizing both—and evident in the more specific sense (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass)—where conceptualizing the malzum alone necessitates conceptualizing the lazim. The latter is what is meant in the definition of iltizamiyya. Thus examples like 'capable of knowledge' or 'craft of writing' for 'insan' are incorrect for iltizamiyya because conceptualizing 'insan' does not immediately necessitate conceptualizing those; they are only examples of the more general sense. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

الالتزامية

Checkpoint
A word like 'bayt' (house) is coined for a dwelling. If used to indicate only the roof, what type of signification is that?
A) Mutabiqiyya
B) Tadammuniyya
C) Iltizamiyya
D) Aqliyya

Key Takeaway
Verbal conventional signification is either matching the whole, implying a part, or entailing an external necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the type of signification in the following: The word 'salah' is coined for a specific set of acts. When a scholar says 'salah', and someone understands it to mean 'bowing alone', what kind of signification is that? Answer: tadammuniyya.
?
Resolve the following problem: The word 'kitab' (book) is coined for a collection of pages. When used to indicate 'knowledge', is that mutabiqiyya, tadammuniyya, or iltizamiyya? Check if knowledge is part of the coined meaning or an external necessity.

Module 7 · Lesson 25

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
7 min
The Dispute over Tadammuniyya: Logicians vs. Grammarians
Author discusses the two schools on tadammuniyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two positions on intention in tadammuniyya and argue why the logicians' view is correct for logical enumeration.

Core Idea
Logicians count part-indication as signification; grammarians require intention.

The author states that tadammuniyya is when a word indicates a part of its coined meaning. But is that always signification? The logicians say yes if understood, while grammarians require the speaker's intention. This lesson examines the dispute and its consequences for the division of signification.

Guided Path
1
State the two schools
Ahl al-mizan (logicians) do not require intention; ahl al-'arabiyya (grammarians) require it.
2
Understand the logicians' justification
They base significance on understanding, not intention. If a word automatically conveys a part when uttered, it counts as tadammuniyya regardless of intent.
3
Understand the grammarians' justification
They argue that signification must be intentional; accidental part-indication is not an act of signification.
The commentary does not elaborate their argument.
4
Assess the consequence for the enumeration
If the grammarians are right, the logicians' tadammuniyya would not fit any of the three divisions, yet it is included in the divided category (maqsama). Thus the logicians' view preserves the enumeration.
This is the commentator's main argument for siding with the logicians.

Checkpoint
According to the logicians, what is the basis for tadammuniyya?
A) The speaker's intention to convey the part
B) The hearer's understanding of the part
C) The grammatical structure of the sentence
D) The presence of a separate indicator

Key Takeaway
Tadammuniyya is considered signification by logicians because the hearer understands the part, even without speaker intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the word 'hajj' coined for visiting the Kaaba. If someone says 'hajj' and the hearer understands 'standing at Arafat', is that tadammuniyya? Answer according to both schools.
?
Suppose a jurist argues that a word does not signify a part unless the speaker intended it. How would you respond using the logicians' argument from the enumeration?

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
The Necessary Concomitant (Lazim) and Its Two Divisions
Author defines the necessary (lazim) and its kinds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two kinds of evident necessary (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'amm and al-akhass) and identify which is relevant for iltizamiyya.

Core Idea
Iltizamiyya requires the stricter necessity: conceptualizing the coinage alone forces the external meaning.

For iltizamiyya signification, the external meaning must be a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the coined meaning. But not every necessity suffices. The commentator distinguishes two senses of evident necessity: a general one where both must be conceptualized to see the connection, and a specific one where the coined meaning alone suffices. Only the latter works for iltizamiyya.

Guided Path
1
Define the condition for iltizamiyya
The external meaning must be necessarily connected (luzum dhihni) to the coined meaning.
2
Identify the two divisions of lazim
First, customary vs. rational; second, evident in the general sense vs. evident in the specific sense.
The lesson focuses on the second division.
3
Explain evident in the general sense (al-a'amm)
When both the coined meaning (malzum) and the necessary (lazim) are conceptualized, one immediately judges the necessity. Example: 'human' and 'capable of knowledge'.
This is not enough for iltizamiyya.
4
Explain evident in the specific sense (al-akhass)
Conceptualizing the coined meaning alone immediately brings the necessary to mind. Example: 'human' and 'animal'? No, that's part, not external. The text says 'human' does not necessitate 'capable of knowledge' in this sense.
This is the intended condition for iltizamiyya.

فصل: وينبغي أن يعلم أن الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية التي لها العبرة في المحاورات والعلوم على ثلاثة أنحاء: أحدها: المطابقية، وهي أن يدل اللفظ على تمام ما وضع له ذلك اللفظ له كدلالة الإنسان على مجموع الحيوان والناطق، وثانيها: التضمنية، وهي أن يدل اللفظ على جزء المعنى الموضوع له كدلالته على الحيوان فقط، وثالثها: الدلالة الالتزامية، وهي أن لا يدل اللفظ على الموضوع له ولا على جزئه، بل على معنى خارج لازم للموضوع له واللازم هو ما ينتقل الذهن من الموضوع له إليه. وذلك اللفظ له إلخ من حيث إنه وضع له. وإنما قيدناها بهذه الحيثية لدفع الإشكال المشهور: وهو أن اللفظ مثلا كالشمس إذا وضع للملزوم كالجرم النوري، واللازم كالضوء، وأريد اللازم - أي الضوء - من جهة أنه لازم لملزومه الموضوع له - أعني الجرم النوري - يكون الدلالة حينئذ التزامية مع أنه يصدق على هذه الدلالة أنها دلالة اللفظ على تمام ما وضع له فيكون هذه الدلالة مطابقية. ودفعه بأن الدلالة المذكورة وإن كانت دلالة اللفظ على تمام ما وضع له لكنها ليس من حيث إنه تمام ما وضع له بل من حيث إنه لازم لملزومه الموضوع له، فظهر أن ترك الحيثية من مسامحة الماتن. وثانيها إلخ : اعلم أن ههنا أي في دلالة التضمن مذهبين مذهب أهل الميزان، وهم لم يعتبروا في الدلالة القصد، بل الفهم فقط، فدلالة اللفظ الموضوع للمعنى المركب على الأجزاء المفهومة في ضمن المعنى المركب بحيث لم يتعلق القصد بها بالذات تضمنية. وأهل العربية اعتبروا القصد فلا تكون تضمنية عندهم. والحق مذهب أهل الميزان؛ فإن على مذهب أهل العربية يبطل الحصر، فإن الدلالة التضمنية الميزانية لا تدخل في شيء من الدلالات مع أنها داخلة في المقسم. أن لازم إنما اعتبر اللزوم في هذه الدلالة؛ لأنها دلالة اللفظ على ما خرج عن المعنى الموضوع له ولا خفاء في اللفظ لا يدل على كل أمر خارج عنه، فلا بد لدلالته على الخارج من شرط، وهو اللزوم الذهني، أي كون الأمر الخارج لازما لمسمّى اللفظ بحيث يلزم من تصور المسمّى تصوره فإنه لو لم يتحقق هذا الشرط لامتنع فهم الأمر الخارج من اللفظ فلم يكن دالا عليه. واللازم الخ: لما وقع في تعريف دلالة الالتزام ذكر اللازم ولم يعلم معناه قبل ذلك ذكر معناه وقال: "واللازم إلخ" اعلم أيها المتعلم الفطن أوّلا، اللازم على قسمين عرفي وعقلي. وقد بينا معناهما سابقا. أورد المصنف محليه مثالين: الأول للأول، والثاني للثاني. وثانيا أن اللازم أيضا على قسمين: أحدهما: اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم: وهو ما إذا تصور الملزوم واللازم يجزم باللزوم بينهما ضرورة وثانيهما: اللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص: وهو أن يلزم من تصور الملزوم تصور اللازم. وهذا المعنى هو المراد عندهم في تعريف الدلالة الالتزامية. فظهر بهذا أن تمثيل اللازم الواقع في دلالة الالتزام بقابل العلم وصنعة الكتابة لا يصح على مذاقهم؛ لظهور أنه لا يلزم من تصور الإنسان تصور قابل العلم وصنعة الكتابة، بل هما مثالان للازم البين بالمعنى الأعم، فإن من تصور =
Section: And it should be known that verbal conventional signification, which is considered in conversations and sciences, is of three kinds: First: mutabiqiyya (matching), which is that the word indicates the entire meaning for which it was coined, like the indication of 'insan' (human) for the combination of animal and rational. Second: tadammuniyya (implication), which is that the word indicates a part of the meaning for which it was coined, like its indication for 'animal' only. Third: iltizamiyya (entaliment), which is that the word does not indicate the coining-meaning nor its part, but rather an external meaning necessarily concomitant (lazim) to the coining-meaning, and the lāzim is what the mind transfers from the coining-meaning to it. 'That word for it etc.' from the perspective that it is coined for it. And we only qualified it with this perspective to repel the famous problem: that a word like 'shams' (sun) is coined for the entailed thing (al-malzum) like the luminous body, and the necessary (al-lazim) like light, and if the necessary—i.e., light—is intended from the aspect that it is necessary for its entailed thing—i.e., the luminous body—then the signification would be iltizamiyya, while it is also true that this signification is the word's indication of the entire coining-meaning, so it would be mutabiqiyya. The repulsion is that the said signification, although it is the word's indication of the entire coining-meaning, is not from the perspective of being the entire coining-meaning but from the perspective of being necessary for its entailed thing. So it appears that dropping the perspective is a looseness of the author. 'And its second etc.': Know that here—i.e., in tadammun signification—there are two schools: the school of ahl al-mizan (logicians), who did not consider intention in signification but only understanding. So the signification of a word coined for a composite meaning about the parts understood within the composite meaning such that the intention is not specifically attached to them is tadammuniyya. The school of ahl al-'arabiyya (grammarians) considered intention, so it is not tadammuniyya according to them. The truth is the school of ahl al-mizan; for according to the school of ahl al-'arabiyya, the enumeration is invalidated, because the tadammuniyya of the logicians does not enter into any of the divisions while it is included in the divided category. That: necessity (luzum) is only considered in this signification because it is the word's indication of what is outside the coining-meaning, and it is not hidden that the word does not indicate every external thing. So for its indication of the external, a condition is necessary, which is mental necessity (al-luzum al-dhihni), i.e., that the external thing is necessary for the meaning of the word such that the conceptualization of the meaning entails its conceptualization. For if this condition is not realized, understanding the external from the word would be impossible, so it would not be indicative of it. 'And the necessary etc.': Since in the definition of iltizamiyya signification the necessary (lazim) was mentioned and its meaning was not known before, he mentions its meaning and says: 'And the necessary etc.' Know, O intelligent student, first that the necessary is two kinds: customary ('urfi) and rational ('aqli). We have already explained their meanings. The author provided two examples for them: the first for the first, the second for the second. Second, the necessary is also two kinds: one: the necessary evident in the more general sense (al-lazim al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'amm): that when the entailed and the necessary are conceptualized, one judges the necessity between them necessarily. The second: the necessary evident in the more specific sense (al-lazim al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass): that conceptualizing the entailed necessitates conceptualizing the necessary. And this meaning is what is intended in their definition of iltizamiyya signification. So it appears from this that representing the necessary occurring in iltizamiyya signification by 'capable of knowledge' and 'craft of writing' is not correct according to their taste; because it is clear that conceptualizing a human does not necessitate conceptualizing 'capable of knowledge' and 'craft of writing'. Rather, both are examples of the necessary evident in the more general sense, for whoever conceptualizes =

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on the three kinds of verbal conventional signification: mutabiqiyya (matching), tadammuniyya (implication by part), and iltizamiyya (entaliment of a necessary concomitant). The author begins by stating the matn that verbal conventional signification used in conversations and sciences is of three types. First, mutabiqiyya: the word signifies the entire meaning for which it was coined, like 'insan' signifying the combination of animal and rational. Second, tadammuniyya: the word signifies a part of that meaning, like 'insan' signifying 'animal' only. Third, iltizamiyya: the word signifies not the coining-meaning nor its part, but an external meaning that is necessarily concomitant (lazim) to the coining-meaning, and the necessary is that to which the mind transfers from the coining-meaning. The commentary then addresses a famous problem: if a word like 'shams' is coined for the luminous body (malzum), and the necessary (light) is intended, the signification would be iltizamiyya, yet it also fits mutabiqiyya since the word indicates its entire coining-meaning? The resolution is that the perspectival qualification (al-haythiyya) must be considered: the signification is iltizamiyya not from the perspective of indicating the entire coining-meaning, but from the perspective of the necessary relation. The author's omission of this qualification in the definition is merely a looseness (musamaha). Next, the commentary discusses tadammuniyya, noting two schools: ahl al-mizan (logicians) who do not require intention (qasd) for signification—only understanding suffices—so a word coined for a composite meaning implies its parts regardless of intention. Ahl al-'arabiyya (grammarians) require intention, so for them such implication is not tadammuniyya. The commentator sides with the logicians, arguing that the grammarians' view invalidates the enumeration (hijr) because the logicians' tadammuniyya would not fit any of the divisions while being part of the divided category (maqsama). Then the commentary explains why necessity (luzum) is crucial for iltizamiyya: the word does not indicate every external thing, so a mental necessity is required—the external must be such that conceptualizing the coining-meaning entails conceptualizing the necessary. Without this, the word could not indicate it. Finally, the commentary defines the necessary (lazim) in two binary divisions: (1) customary vs. rational, with examples given; (2) evident in the more general sense (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'amm)—where one judges the necessity immediately upon conceptualizing both—and evident in the more specific sense (al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-akhass)—where conceptualizing the malzum alone necessitates conceptualizing the lazim. The latter is what is meant in the definition of iltizamiyya. Thus examples like 'capable of knowledge' or 'craft of writing' for 'insan' are incorrect for iltizamiyya because conceptualizing 'insan' does not immediately necessitate conceptualizing those; they are only examples of the more general sense. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

Key Terms
اللازم
اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم
اللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

The Necessary Concomitant: Defining and Distinguishing Two Types of Evident Lazim
Commentator defines lazim and divides it into two types: bayyin bi ma'na a'amm and akhass.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of evident lazim (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm and bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass) and identify which type is used in the definition of concomitant signification.

Core Idea
The specific evident lazim alone grounds concomitant signification; the general sense only gives judgment.

In this passage, the commentator defines the necessary concomitant (lazim) that appears in the definition of concomitant signification. He explains that mental concomitance is the condition for a word to signify an external meaning. Then he divides evident lazim into two types: the general sense, where you judge the necessity only after conceiving both terms, and the specific sense, where the lazim springs to mind immediately from the malzum alone. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying concomitant signification in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term and condition
The commentator begins by explaining why lazim is needed: because words signify external meanings only under the condition of mental concomitance (luzum dhihni). Your task: locate in the Arabic where this condition is stated and what it entails.
The condition is that conceiving the malzum leads to conceiving the lazim.
2
Find the two main divisions of lazim
The commentator says lazim is first divided into customary and rational (which were explained earlier). Then he mentions the more important division: lazim bayyin (evident) which comes in two varieties. Identify the Arabic terms used for these divisions.
The two are: al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm and al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass.
3
Define the first type: bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm
According to the passage, this is when upon conceiving both the malzum and the lazim, one judges necessarily that there is a relation of concomitance. Your task: paraphrase this definition in your own words and note what mental operation is required.
The key is that you need to conceive both before making the judgment.
4
Define the second type: bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass
This is when the conception of the malzum alone necessitates the conception of the lazim. Compare this to the first type: what additional property does the specific type have?
It automatically comes to mind; no separate conception of the lazim is needed.
5
Apply the distinction to the given example
The commentator criticizes the example of 'human' signifying 'capable of knowledge' or 'craft of writing'. Determine whether this is an example of the general or specific type according to the passage. Explain why in your own words.
It is only general because one does not automatically think of 'capable of knowledge' from 'human' alone; one must also conceive that property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لازم إنما اعتبر اللزوم في هذه الدلالة؛ لأنها دلالة اللفظ على ما خرج عن المعنى الموضوع له ولا خفاء في اللفظ لا يدل على كل أمر خارج عنه، فلا بد لدلالته على الخارج من شرط، وهو اللزوم الذهني، أي كون الأمر الخارج لازما لمسمّى اللفظ بحيث يلزم من تصور المسمّى تصوره فإنه لو لم يتحقق هذا الشرط لامتنع فهم الأمر الخارج من اللفظ فلم يكن دالا عليه. واللازم الخ: لما وقع في تعريف دلالة الالتزام ذكر اللازم ولم يعلم معناه قبل ذلك ذكر معناه وقال: 'واللازم إلخ' اعلم أيها المتعلم الفطن أوّلا، اللازم على قسمين عرفي وعقلي. وقد بينا معناهما سابقا. أورد المصنف محليه مثالين: الأول للأول، والثاني للثاني. وثانيا أن اللازم أيضا على قسمين: أحدهما: اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم: وهو ما إذا تصور الملزوم واللازم يجزم باللزوم بينهما ضرورة وثانيهما: اللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص: وهو أن يلزم من تصور الملزوم تصور اللازم. وهذا المعنى هو المراد عندهم في تعريف الدلالة الالتزامية. فظهر بهذا أن تمثيل اللازم الواقع في دلالة الالتزام بقابل العلم وصنعة الكتابة لا يصح على مذاقهم؛ لظهور أنه لا يلزم من تصور الإنسان تصور قابل العلم وصنعة الكتابة، بل هما مثالان للازم البين بالمعنى الأعم، فإن من تصور مفهوم الإنسان وتصور مفهوم قابل العلم وصنعة الكتابة يجزم باللزوم بينهما فاحفظ يجديك نفعا في الاعتراض الآتي من الإمام. كدلالة إلخ فإن قلت البصر جزء مفهوم العمى؛ فإن العمي عدم البصر، فلا يكون دلالته عليه بالالتزام بل بالتضمن؟ فنقول : العمى عدم البصر لا العدم والبصر والعدم المضاف إلى البصر يكون البصر خارجا عنه وإلا لاجتمع في العمى البصر وعدمه.
[The word] 'lazim': They only considered concomitance in this signification because it is the signification of the word on what is outside the meaning placed for, and it is not hidden that the word does not signify every external thing. So there must be a condition for its signification on the external, which is mental concomitance (al-luzum al-dhihni), i.e., that the external thing is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the name of the word such that the conception of the name necessitates the conception of it. For if this condition were not realized, understanding the external thing from the word would be impossible, so it would not be significative of it. And the necessary concomitant (etc.): Since in the definition of concomitant signification the term 'lazim' occurred, and its meaning was not known before that, he mentioned its meaning and said: 'And the lazim etc.' Know, O intelligent student, first that lazim is of two types: customary and rational. We have explained their meanings previously. The author gave two examples for them: the first for the first, the second for the second. And second, that lazim is also of two types: one: the evident lazim in the general sense (al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm), which is that when one conceives the malzum and the lazim, one judges necessarily the concomitance between them. And second: the evident lazim in the specific sense (al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass), which is that the conception of the malzum necessitates the conception of the lazim. And this meaning is what is meant by them in the definition of concomitant signification. So it appears from this that exemplifying the lazim occurring in concomitant signification with 'capable of knowledge' and 'craft of writing' is not valid according to their taste; because it is clear that the conception of 'human' does not necessitate the conception of 'capable of knowledge' or 'craft of writing'. Rather, they are examples of the evident lazim in the general sense. Because one who conceives the concept of human and conceives the concept of capable of knowledge or craft of writing judges necessarily the concomitance between them. So memorize, it will benefit you in the upcoming objection from the Imam. And like the signification etc. If you say: sight is part of the concept of blindness; because blindness is the absence of sight, so its signification upon it would be implicative, not concomitant? We say: Blindness is the absence of sight, not the absence and sight. And the absence added to sight makes sight external to it, otherwise blindness would combine sight and its absence.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the necessary concomitant (lazim) used in concomitant signification. The commentator explains that mental concomitance is required: the external meaning must be such that conceiving the original meaning necessitates conceiving it. Otherwise, the word cannot signify that external thing. Then the commentator divides lazim: first into customary and rational (which he says were explained earlier), then into evident in the general sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm) and evident in the specific sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass). The general sense is when one judges the concomitance necessarily upon conceiving both; the specific sense is when conceiving the malzum alone necessitates conceiving the lazim. The specific sense is what is meant in the definition of iltizam. The commentator then critiques the example of 'human' signifying 'capable of knowledge' or 'craft of writing'—these are only evident in the general sense, not the specific, because one does not automatically conceive them just from conceiving 'human'. They are simply examples of the general sense. The passage ends with a separate objection about the word 'blindness' signifying 'sight'. Someone objects that sight is part of the concept of blindness (since blindness is absence of sight), so it should be implicative, not concomitant. The commentator responds that blindness is the absence of sight, so sight is external to the concept of blindness, not a part; otherwise, blindness would entail sight and its absence simultaneously, which is contradictory.

Key Terms
اللازم (al-lazim): A meaning that is necessarily connected to another meaning such that conception of the other necessitates conception of it (in specific sense) or at least judgment of necessity (in general sense).
اللزوم الذهني (al-luzum al-dhihni): Mental concomitance: the condition that conceiving the malzum leads to conceiving the lazim.
البين بالمعنى الأعم (al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm): Evident in the general sense: a lazim that one judges to be necessary when both are conceived, but does not automatically come to mind from the malzum alone.
البين بالمعنى الأخص (al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass): Evident in the specific sense: a lazim that is automatically conceived when one conceives the malzum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the example of 'human' signifying 'capable of knowledge' not considered a valid example of concomitant signification according to the logicians?
A) Because 'capable of knowledge' is a part of the meaning of 'human'.
B) Because it is an example of implicative signification.
C) Because conceiving 'human' does not automatically necessitate conceiving 'capable of knowledge'; it is only evident in the general sense.
D) Because 'human' and 'capable of knowledge' are not related.

Key Takeaway
Concomitant signification requires the specific evident lazim, where the malzum alone yields the lazim in the mind, not just a judgment after conceiving both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For each of the following pairs from Islamic sciences, determine whether the lazim is bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass or bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm, and explain your reasoning using the criterion of automatic conception: (a) 'nabiy' (prophet) and 'isma' (infallibility). (b) 'insan' (human) and 'qabul al-'ilm' (capability of knowledge).
?
The commentator argues that the example of 'human' signifying 'capable of knowledge' does not count as concomitant signification. Reconstruct his argument in your own words, using the distinction between the two types of evident lazim.

What Is Mantiq?

Implicative Signification: General or Specific?
Author critiques Ibn Sina's view on implicative signification, arguing the necessary relation is general not specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that implicative signification includes general necessary concomitants, and explain why it follows that implicative signification is inseparable from conformative signification.

Core Idea
Implicative signification includes every necessary concomitant, not just specific or clear ones.

This passage challenges a common restriction in the definition of implicative signification (dalālah iltizāmiyyah). The author points out that the logicians' own examples, such as 'human' signifying 'capacity for knowledge', involve a general concomitant. By analyzing their inconsistency, the author concludes that implicative signification must include all necessary concomitants, even the most basic like 'not being other than itself'. This clarification is vital for understanding how words convey multiple layers of meaning in Islamic legal and theological texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' definition
Recall that the standard definition of implicative signification is the signification of a word on a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of its literal meaning. Note that the author claims they did not restrict it to being 'clear' or 'specific'.
The logicians' definition appears earlier in the text; here the author is critiquing it.
2
Find the counterexample from their own work
The author points out that the logicians themselves gave the example of 'human' (insān) signifying 'capacity for knowledge' (qābilīyat al-ilm) as implicative signification. Analyze why this is a general concomitant, not a specific one.
Capacity for knowledge is a general property shared by all humans, not a unique differentia.
3
Expose the inconsistency
If they accept that 'human' implies 'capacity for knowledge' (a general concomitant), then they must also accept that 'human' implies 'not being other than itself' (an even more basic concomitant). The author argues that the latter is even lower in degree of necessity, so it should be included.
The author uses a fortiori reasoning: if a weaker implication is admitted, a stronger one must also be admitted.
4
Conclude the generalization
Since the logicians did not explicitly restrict necessity to the specific sense, and they used a general example, the author concludes that they intended the general sense. Therefore, implicative signification covers all necessary concomitants, not just specific or clear ones.
This conclusion undermines the common view that implicative signification is limited.
5
Derive the inseparability from conformative signification
Because every word has at least the necessary concomitant of 'not being other than itself', implicative signification is always present. Hence, it cannot be separated from conformative signification (mutābaqah), which signifies the literal meaning.
This means that every word simultaneously has both types of signification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية المفرد والمركب 11 وقولك: ليس "غيره ليس من اللوازم البينة؛ لأنا كثيرا ما نتصور المعاني ولا يخطر ببالنا معنى الغير فضلا عن كونه ليس غيره. فصل: اللفظ الدال إما مفرد وإما مركب فالمفرد ما لا يقصد بجزئه الدلالة على جزء معناه كدلالة همزة الاستفهام على معناه ودلالة زيد على مسمّاه، ودلالة على المعنى العلمي. والمركب ما يقصد بجزئه الدلالة على جزء معناه، الله عبد : كبرائهم من ابن سينا وأمثاله - يدلك دلالة واضحة على الأعم كما نبهناك سابقا. هو أن المعتبر عندهم في التعريف هو اللازم البين بالمعنى والإمام - قدس الله سره - إنما يخاطب الشيخ ونظراءه بطريق الإلزام بأنكم فسرتم الدلالة الالتزامية بدلالة اللفظ على لازم ما وضع له وما قيدتموه بشيء لا إشارة ولا صراحة، بل قد اعترفتم بكون الإنسان دالا على قابلية العلم وصنعة الكتابة دلالة التزامية مع كونه لازما للإنسان بالمعنى الأعم لا بالمعنى الأخص كما ذكرنا؛ فحينئذ كيف يسوغ لكم إنكار دلالة كل لفظ على كون مفهومه ليس" "غيره فإن كون الإنسان مثلا ليس غيره أدون درجة وأنزل مرتبة في اللزوم من كونه قابلا للعلم وصنعة الكتابة؟ وبالجملة التمثيل بقابل العلم وصنعة الكتابة وعدم تصريحهم بكون المعتبر اللزوم بالمعنى الأخص يخبر بكون التعميم في اللزوم مرادا عندهم، وعلى هذا فلا يمكن انفكاك الالتزام من المطابقة كما ذكره الإمام - قدس سره - فاحفظه وتنبه له، لعلك لا تجده عند غيرنا، والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
Linguistic conventional signification, the single word and the compound, 11. And your statement: 'not other than it is not among the clear necessary implications, because we often conceive meanings and the meaning of 'other' does not occur to our minds, let alone its being not other than it.' Section: The signifying word is either single or compound. The single is that whose part is not intended to signify a part of its meaning, like the signification of the interrogative hamza on its meaning and the signification of 'Zaid' on its referent, and the signification of the conceptual meaning. The compound is that whose part is intended to signify a part of its meaning, like 'Abdullah'. Their leaders like Ibn Sina and his likes indicate clearly the general meaning as we previously alerted you. That which is considered by them in definition is the clear necessary implication in the sense that the Imam (may Allah sanctify his spirit) only addresses the Shaykh and his peers by way of obligation: You explained implicative signification as the signification of a word on a necessary concomitant of what it is posited for, and you did not restrict it with anything, neither indication nor explicitness. Rather, you acknowledged that 'human' signifies 'capacity for knowledge and the craft of writing' with implicative signification, even though it is a necessary concomitant of 'human' in the general sense, not the specific sense, as we mentioned. Then how is it permissible for you to deny that every word signifies that its concept is not other than it? Indeed, 'human' for example is not other than it; it is lower in degree and rank in necessity than its being capable of knowledge and the craft of writing. In summary, their example of the capacity for knowledge and the craft of writing, and their lack of explicit statement that the considered necessity is the specific sense, informs that generalization in necessity is intended by them. On this basis, implicative signification cannot be separated from conformative signification, as the Imam (may Allah sanctify his spirit) mentioned. So memorize it and be attentive to it; maybe you will not find it with other than us. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the logicians' view on implicative signification (al-dalālah al-iltizāmiyyah). The author begins by stating that a word's signification is either single or compound, then immediately launches into a critique of the position held by Ibn Sīnā and his followers. The issue is about the condition of the necessary relation (al-lāzim) in implicative signification: they earlier defined it as signification of a necessary concomitant of the meaning, but they did not restrict it to being 'clear' (bayyin) or explicit. The author points out that they themselves gave examples like 'human' signifying 'capacity for knowledge' – which is a general necessary concomitant, not a specific one. So how can they deny that every word signifies that its concept is not other than itself? That is an even more basic and obvious concomitant. The author argues that the lack of restriction in their definition shows that they intend generalization in necessity. Therefore, implicative signification cannot be detached from conformative signification (al-mutābaqah), as the Imam (al-Rāzī?) stated. The tone is urgent: 'memorize it and be attentive, maybe you will not find it with others.' This is typical of commentaries that correct a misunderstanding in the core texts.

What Is Mantiq?

The Logical Verb (Kalimah) vs. the Grammatical Verb (Fi'l)
Author refutes the mistaken identification of logical verb (kalimah) with grammatical verb (fi'l).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given Arabic verb qualifies as a logical kalimah by checking its composition and time association.

Core Idea
The logical kalimah is narrower than the grammatical fi'l – it excludes compound verbs and verbal nouns.

A common error among beginners in Islamic logic is to assume that every grammatical verb (fi'l) is also a logical word (kalimah). However, logicians define kalimah more restrictively: it must be a simple word whose meaning is primarily associated with a specific time (past, present, or future). In this lesson, we will dissect the argument of the author, supported by Ibn Sīnā and al-Ṭūsī, to clearly distinguish these terms, a distinction crucial for analyzing propositions in Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
Locate the passage where the author states that some have wrongly identified kalimah with the grammatical fi'l. Understand that this is the error being corrected.
2
Extract the definition of logical kalimah
From the description, list the three conditions: (1) the word must be simple (mufrad), (2) its meaning must be associated with one of the three times (past, present, future), (3) this association must be primary (iqtirān awwalī) according to linguistic convention. Excludes verbal nouns and compound forms.
Primary association means the time is part of the word's core meaning, not derived from context.
3
Apply the definition to problematic cases
Test specific examples: first- and second-person imperfect verbs like 'amshī' (I walk) are compound (include pronoun subject) and therefore not kalimah. Verbal nouns like 'ṣah' (silence!) have time association but not primarily – they are excluded by the 'iqtirān awwalī' condition.
Ibn Sīnā argued that non-third-person imperfects are compound; al-Ṭūsī confirmed this.
4
Generalize the distinction
Conclude that the grammatical fi'l is broader: it includes both simple and compound verbs. The logical kalimah is only the simple verb (primarily the third person form) whose time reference is intrinsic. This prevents errors in logical analysis of temporal statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Section: Know that some have supposed that the word 'kalimah' according to the logicians is what is called in grammar 'fi'l' (verb), but this supposition is not correct. Indeed, the verb is more general than the kalimah. Do you not see that ... 4. So these four are included in the single, except that the author in his place was lenient in encompassing the divisions, so he did not mention the last among the single even though it is included in it. 'If it is associated with it, etc.' means its meaning is associated with a particular time from the three times. Thus excluded is what is not associated with a particular time from the three times, whether it is not associated with any time at all like other nouns, or it is associated with a particular time other than the three, like 'ṣabūḥ' (morning drink) and 'ghabūq' (evening drink) and their likes. And what is meant by the association of meaning in the definition of kalimah is that its meaning is associated with a specific time from the three times, a primary association according to the convention (waḍ‘), so that it is not refuted by verbal nouns (asmā’ al-af‘āl) because their meaning is also associated with a specific time from the three times, like 'ṣah' (silence) which indicates silence associated with the future, and so that the refutation by active and passive participles (ism al-fā‘il and ism al-maf‘ūl) is not directed, because they are not associated with a primary association according to convention. 'So it is a tool, etc.' As for calling it a tool (adah), it is because it is an instrument in combining one word with another, and the tool is the intermediary. As for the reason for naming the second division 'kalimah' and the first 'ism', it is because 'kalimah' comes from 'kalīm' which means wound, as if because it indicates time which is renewing and passing, the mind speaks of the change of its meaning, and that the noun is higher in rank than other types of words, so it contains the meaning of 'sumuww' which is highness. Section etc.: When in the previous section it was mentioned that the single has divisions including the kalimah, and some mistakenly thought that the logical kalimah and the grammatical fi'l are identical in concept, this section refutes it, saying: indeed, it has been supposed etc. This supposition is not correct. The Shaykh stated in 'al-Shifā': That which the Arabs call a verb is not a kalimah according to the logicians; because the non-third-person imperfect (i.e., first and second person) is a verb in grammar but is not a kalimah. As for it being a verb, it is obvious; as for it not being a kalimah, it is because the second-person imperfect and likewise the first-person are compound, and nothing compound is a kalimah. So no second-person or first-person imperfect is a kalimah. Indeed, the verb is more general. Al-Muḥaqqiq al-Ṭūsī explicitly stated in 'Sharḥ al-Ishārāt' that the verb in grammar is more general than it is in logic, because they (logicians) consider words composed with pronouns like 'amshī' (I walk) as not kalimah.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement correcting a common mistake: equating the logical 'kalimah' with the grammatical 'fi'l'. The author begins by stating that some people mistakenly think so, but it is incorrect because fi'l is more general. He then explains the correct definition of kalimah: its meaning must be primarily associated with one of the three times (past, present, future). This excludes verbal nouns (asmā’ al-af‘āl) like 'ṣah' (silence) because they are associated with a specific time but not primarily; and also excludes active/passive participles because their time association is not primary. The reason for the name 'kalimah' is derived from 'kalīm' (wound), indicating change associated with time. The author then references Ibn Sīnā's 'al-Shifā' and al-Ṭūsī's 'Sharḥ al-Ishārāt' to support his point: first/second person imperfect verbs (like 'amshī' – I walk) are compound in logic because they include the pronoun subject, so they are not kalimah; only the third person imperfect without pronoun is kalimah. Thus, the grammatical verb includes both compound and simple forms, while the logical verb excludes compound ones. The passage also includes a digression returning to the earlier discussion about the four types of mufrad, but that may be a textual artifact; the core argument is the distinction between kalimah and fi'l.

The Distinction Between Simple and Compound Expressions in Logic
Author explains why certain verb forms are considered compound by logicians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between mufrad and murakkab in the logical sense and apply this classification to Arabic verb forms.

Core Idea
A verb with an integrated pronoun is a sentence, not a word, for logicians.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key terminological distinction between grammarians and logicians. While grammarians treat all verb forms as simple words, logicians analyze whether part of the word indicates part of the meaning. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly parsing propositions in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms mufrad and murakkab
Define mufrad as a word whose parts do not convey partial meanings, and murakkab as an expression where part of the utterance indicates part of the meaning.
In logic, mufrad includes simple nouns, verbs, and particles that are not further analyzable into meaningful parts.
2
Examine verb forms أضرب and يضرب
Focus on 'aḍribu' (I strike) and 'yaḍribu' (he strikes) as given in the passage. Note that 'aḍribu' contains an integrated pronoun (the hamza for 'I') while 'yaḍribu' does not specify an agent.
3
Identify why أضرب is murakkab
The hamza (أ) indicates the speaker, so part of the word (the prefix) conveys a part of the overall meaning (agent). This violates the condition for mufrad, making 'aḍribu' a compound expression.
The same applies to 'naḍribu' where the nūn (ن) indicates 'we'.
4
Contrast with يضرب
'Yaḍribu' lacks any integrated pronoun; it merely denotes the event without specifying the agent. Therefore, it remains mufrad and is considered a 'kalima' by logicians.
If an agent is mentioned explicitly (like Zayd), the expression becomes a compound sentence, but the verb itself stays simple.
5
Apply the rule to other verb forms
Consider 'taḍribu' (you strike) – the tā (ت) indicates the addressee, so it is also compound. Recognize that any verb with an integrated pronoun for the agent is murakkab for logicians.
This classification affects whether an utterance is considered a full proposition (qadiyya) or a simple term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَضْرِبُ ونَضْرِبُ وأمثاله فعل عند النحاة وليس بكلمة عند المنطقيين؛ لأن الكلمة من أقسام المفرد، ونحو "أضرب" مثلا ليس بمفرد، بل هو مركب؛ لدلالة جزء اللفظ على جزء المعنى، فإن الهمزة تدل على المتكلم، و "التاء" على المخاطب، و "النون" على المتكلم، والباقي على الحدث؛ فلذا عَدُّوهما من المركبات التامة الخبرية وأخرجوا عن الكلمة، بخلاف "يضرب" فإنه كلمة عند المنطقيين والعرب كليهما؛ إذ لا يفهم منه معنى المركب، والقضية ما لم يصرح بالفاعل، فإن "يضرب بلا ذكر الفاعل لا يفيد معنى يضرب أحد أو زيد" ولا يلزم عند ذكرهما التأكيد وهو باطل قطعا في محاوراتهم نعم في المخاطب عند ذكر الفاعل مثل تضرب "أنت" وفي المتكلم عند ذكره نحو: "أضرب أنا، ونضرب نحن" يفهم التأكيد قطعا في المحاورات فوضح الفرق.
I strike and we strike and their likes are verbs according to grammarians but are not a word (kalima) according to logicians; because the word is one of the divisions of the single (mufrad), and like 'aḍribu' for example is not single (mufrad), but is compound; because part of the utterance indicates part of the meaning, for 'hamza' indicates the speaker, and 'tā' indicates the addressee, and 'nūn' indicates the speaker, and the remainder indicates the event; therefore they counted them among the complete declarative compounds and excluded them from word (kalima), unlike 'yaḍribu' for it is a word according to both logicians and Arabs; since no compound meaning is understood from it, and the proposition (qadiyya) unless the agent is explicitly stated, for 'yaḍribu' without mentioning the agent does not convey the meaning of 'someone strikes' or 'Zayd strikes', and when mentioning them, emphasis (tawkīd) is not necessitated, and that is absolutely false in their speech; yes, in the addressee when mentioning the agent such as 'taḍribu anta' and in the speaker when mentioning it such as 'aḍribu anā' and 'naḍribu naḥnu', emphasis is understood absolutely in speech; so the difference became clear.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage explains why certain Arabic verb forms are considered compound (murakkab) by logicians, even though grammarians classify them as simple verbs. The author begins by stating that verbs like 'aḍribu' (I strike) and 'naḍribu' (we strike) are considered verbs by grammarians but not 'kalima' (word) by logicians. The reason is that 'kalima' is a type of mufrad (single word), but these forms are not mufrad because part of the utterance indicates part of the meaning: the hamza (أ) indicates the speaker (first person), the tā (ت) indicates the addressee (second person), and the nūn (ن) indicates the speaker (first person plural), while the root 'ḍ-r-b' indicates the event. Thus they are complete declarative compounds (murakkabāt tāmma khabariyya), i.e., sentences. In contrast, 'yaḍribu' (he strikes) is considered a word by both logicians and Arabs because it does not convey a compound meaning; it only indicates the event without specifying the agent. The author clarifies that mentioning the agent explicitly (like 'Zayd' or 'someone') with 'yaḍribu' does not create a compound; it simply clarifies. However, with 'aḍribu' or 'naḍribu', if the pronoun is restated (e.g., 'aḍribu anā'), it becomes emphatic, proving the pronoun is already implied. Thus the distinction is clear: verb forms with integrated pronouns are compound in logic, while impersonal forms are simple.

Key Terms
مفرد
مركب
كلمة
قضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because 'أضرب' is a longer word.
C) Because grammarians said so.
D) Because 'يضرب' is a preposition.

Key Takeaway
A verb form is compound in logic if it contains an integrated pronoun that specifies the agent; otherwise it is simple.

Actually 'أقم' is a command verb with an implicit 'you' subject, but the form 'أقم' does not have a visible pronoun like 'أضرب'. Examine whether the logicians would consider 'أقم' as murakkab or mufrad, and compare with 'أضرب'.

Complete and Incomplete Compounds in Logic
Author defines complete and incomplete compound with examples and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic expression as a complete (tāmm) or incomplete (nāqiṣ) compound based on the criterion of self-sufficiency in speech.

Core Idea
A complete compound stands alone; an incomplete compound leaves you waiting.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational classification of compound expressions (murakkab) in Islamic logic. The author distinguishes between two types: complete and incomplete. This distinction is crucial for understanding how propositions are formed, which are the building blocks of arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the passage and note that the author divides compound (murakkab) into two categories: complete (tāmm) and incomplete (nāqiṣ).
2
Understand the criterion
The criterion for completeness is whether one can be silent after uttering the expression without needing more. This is the key test.
This is a pragmatic linguistic test, not a grammatical one.
3
Apply the test to the example
The example 'Zayd is standing' satisfies the test: you can stop there. Contrast with 'Zayd's' which leaves you waiting for more.
The example uses a subject-predicate sentence.
4
Classify new expressions
Take other Arabic expressions, such as 'the world is originated' (complete) and 'in the house' (incomplete), and verify using the same test.
Practice is essential to internalize the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: المركب قسمان: أحدهما: المركب التام، وهو ما يصح السكوت عليه كزيد قائم، وثانيهما: المركب الناقص، هو ما ليس كذلك.
Chapter: The compound is two divisions: one of them is the complete compound, which is what it is permissible to be silent upon, like 'Zayd is standing', and the second is the incomplete compound, which is not like that.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines the two divisions of compound (murakkab) in logic. The author begins by stating that compound is of two types. The first is the complete compound (al-murakkab al-tamm), which is a phrase that can stand alone in discourse, such that one may be silent after uttering it without feeling a need for more words. The example given is 'Zayd is standing' (Zaidun qāʾimun). The second is the incomplete compound (al-murakkab al-nāqiṣ), which does not satisfy this condition; it leaves the listener waiting for completion, like 'Zayd's' or 'in the house'. This classification is foundational for understanding sentences and phrases in logic.

Key Terms
المركب (al-murakkab): A linguistic expression consisting of two or more words. [Note: In logic, murakkab is any composite expression; in grammar it specifically refers to phrases.]
المركب التام (al-murakkab al-tāmm): A compound that is self-sufficient, it conveys a complete meaning and can be used to end a speech.
المركب الناقص (al-murakkab al-nāqiṣ): A compound that is not self-sufficient; it requires something else to complete the meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 'The world is originated'
C) 'In the house'
D) 'If Zayd comes'

Key Takeaway
The distinction between complete and incomplete compound is based on whether the expression can stand alone in speech; this classification is essential for identifying valid propositions in logic.

Particular and Universal: The Condition of Mere Conception
Author defines the concept and divides it into particular and universal, clarifying the condition of 'self of conception'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between particular and universal concepts by applying the condition of mere conception.

Core Idea
The distinction hinges on whether mere conception prevents multiple instantiations, not external reality.

The author defines the concept (al-mafhum) as what is obtained in the mind, then divides it into particular and universal. The key to this division is the subtle condition 'mere conception' (nafs al-tasawwur). Without it, some universals like the Necessary Existent or hypothetical concepts would be wrongly classified as particulars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined (al-mafhum)
Locate the definiendum: 'al-mafhum' is defined as 'what is obtained in the mind'. Note that this is a broad definition covering both actual and potential mental content.
2
Extract the definitions of particular and universal
Find the two definitions: particular is 'that whose mere conception prevents being true of many' (e.g., Zayd, this horse); universal is 'that whose mere conception does not prevent sharing and being true of many' (e.g., human, horse).
Both definitions include the phrase 'nafs tasawwurihi' (its mere conception).
3
Analyze the condition 'mere conception'
Understand why this condition is added: without it, universals like Wajib al-Wujud (Necessary Existent) would be considered particulars because they cannot be many externally. But their concept does not logically preclude multiplicity; hence they are universals. Similarly, 'la-shay' (not-a-thing) has no external instances but its concept can be shared in thought.
The condition ensures that the definition focuses on the concept itself, not on external facts.
4
Test the definitions with examples
Apply the definitions to Wajib al-Wujud and 'la-shay'. Since their mere conception does not prevent being predicated of many (in thought), they are universals. If the condition were omitted, they would wrongly be classified as particulars.
This shows the logical necessity of the condition for a correct classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: المفهوم أي ما حصل في الذهن قسمان: أحدهما جزئي، والثاني كلي. أما الجزئي: فهو ما يمنع نفس تصوره عن صدقه على كثيرين، كزيد، وعمرو، وهذا الفرس، وهذا الجدار. وأما الكلي فهو ما لا يمنع نفس تصوره عن وقوع الشركة فيه، وعن صدقه على كثيرين كالإنسان والفرس.

فصل: لما فرغ عن بيان الدلالة وأقسام اللفظ شرع في بيان الكليات فقال: المفهوم إلخ. المفهوم: أي: ما من شأنه أن يحصل في الذهن سواء كان حاصلا بالفعل أو لا واعلم أن الكلية والجزئية بالذات إنما هي صفة للمعنى دون اللفظ لكن يتصف بهما اللفظ تبعا تسمية الدال باسم المدلول، كما أن الإفراد والتركيب صفتان للألفاظ دون المعاني لكن يتصف بهما المعاني تبعا تسمية المدلول باسم الدال. كلي وجه التسمية بالكلي والجزئي أن الكلي جزء للجزئي غالبا كالحيوان؛ فإنه جزء لكل واحد من أفراده، وكالإنسان؛ فإنه جزء لكل واحد من أفراده؛ فيكون الجزئي كلا والكلي جزءً، فالكلي لما كان منسوبا إلى الكل أي: الجزئي سمي كليا؛ لأن المنسوب إلى الكل كلي وكذا المنسوب إلى الجزء يعني الكلي جزئي، فاحفظ نفس تصوره: إنما قيد بنفس التصور؛ لأن من الكليات ما يمنع الشركة بالنظر إلى الخارج كواجب الوجود؛ فإن الشركة فيه ممتنعة بالدليل الخارجي، لكن إذا جرّد العقل بالنظر إلى مفهومه لم يمنع من صدقه على كثيرين؛ فإن مجرد تصوره لو كان مانعا من الشركة لم يفتقر في إثبات الوحدانية إلى دليل آخر وكالكليات الفرضية، مثلا اللاشيء وغيره فإنها يمتنع أن تصدق على شيء من الأشياء في الخارج لكن لا بالنظر إلى مجرد تصورها. فلو لم يعتبر نفس التصور في تعريف الكلي والجزئي لدخلت تلك الكليات في تعريف الجزئي، فلا يكون مانعا، وخرجت عن تعريف الكلي فلا يكون جامعا.
Chapter: The concept (i.e., what is obtained in the mind) is of two divisions: one is particular, and the second is universal. As for the particular: it is that whose mere conception prevents it from being true of many, like Zayd, Amr, this horse, and this wall. As for the universal: it is that whose mere conception does not prevent sharing in it nor being true of many, like human and horse.

Chapter: When he finished explaining signification and the divisions of the word, he began explaining the universals and said: the concept, etc. The concept: i.e., that which is apt to be obtained in the mind, whether it is actually obtained or not. Know that universality and particularity in essence are attributes of the meaning, not the word, but the word acquires them secondarily by naming the signifier after the signified, just as singularity and composition are attributes of words, not meanings, but meanings acquire them secondarily by naming the signified after the signifier. The reason for naming the universal and particular: the universal is a part of the particular most often, like animal—for it is a part of each of its individuals, and like human—for it is a part of each of its individuals; thus the particular is a whole and the universal is a part. So because the universal is related to the whole (i.e., the particular), it is called universal; for that which is related to the whole is universal, and similarly that which is related to the part (i.e., the universal) is particular. So preserve [the condition of] 'its mere conception': it is qualified by 'mere conception' because among universals there are those whose sharing is prevented with respect to the external, like the Necessary Existent—sharing in it is prevented by an external proof, but if the intellect abstracts and looks to its concept, it does not prevent it from being true of many; for if its mere conception were a preventer of sharing, there would be no need for another proof to establish oneness. And similarly among hypothetical universals, like not-a-thing and others, it is impossible for them to be true of anything externally, but not with respect to their mere conception. So if the mere conception were not considered in the definition of universal and particular, those universals would enter into the definition of particular, and thus it would not be exclusive, and they would exit from the definition of universal, and thus it would not be comprehensive.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section from a commentary on a text about the concept (al-mafhum). The author begins by defining the concept as 'what is obtained in the mind' and divides it into two: particular (juz'i) and universal (kulli). The particular is defined as that whose mere conception (nafs tasawwurihi) prevents it from being true of many individuals—examples given are proper names (Zayd, Amr) and demonstratives (this horse, this wall). The universal is defined as that whose mere conception does not prevent multiple instantiations and being true of many, exemplified by 'human' and 'horse'.

Then the commentary expands on several key points. First, it clarifies that universality and particularity are essentially attributes of meanings (concepts), not words, but words can be described as universal or particular secondarily because the signifier (the word) is named after the signified (the meaning). Conversely, singularity and composition (i.e., being a single word vs. compound) are primarily attributes of words, but meanings can be described as singular or compound secondarily. 

The commentary then explains the etymology of 'kulli' (universal): because the universal is a part of the particular (the whole) in most cases—for example, 'animal' is a part of each individual animal, and 'human' is a part of each individual human—so the universal is related to the whole (the particular), thus called 'kulli' (pertaining to the whole). The particular is related to the part (the universal), thus called 'juz'i' (pertaining to the part).

The most important clarification is the inclusion of 'nafs tasawwurihi' (its mere conception) in the definitions. Without this condition, the definitions would fail. For instance, the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) is necessarily one in external reality, but its mere concept does not logically preclude multiple instantiations—if it did, we would not need a philosophical proof to establish its oneness. Similarly, hypothetical universals like 'not-a-thing' (la-shay') have no external instances, but their mere concept does not prevent them from being predicated of many in thought. If we did not restrict to 'mere conception', these would wrongly fall under the particular (since they cannot be many externally) and would be excluded from the universal, making the definition of particular non-exclusive and that of universal non-comprehensive.

Key Terms
المفهوم
الجزئي
الكلي
نفس التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To prevent hypothetical universals like 'la-shay' (not-a-thing) from being classified as particulars, because their mere conception does not prevent multiple instantiations even if external reality does.

The condition of mere conception is crucial for correctly classifying concepts as particular or universal, preventing misclassification of concepts that are impossible externally but universal in thought.

Definition of Categorical Proposition and Its Two Types
Author defines categorical proposition and its decomposition into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish categorical from conditional propositions and decompose a categorical proposition into its constituent parts.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition resolves into singles, not propositions.

In this lesson, we define the categorical proposition (al-ḥamliyya) and show how it decomposes either into two single terms or into one single and one proposition. We also address a common objection that some categorical propositions have complex terms. By understanding the distinction between actual and potential singles, you will be able to properly analyze propositional structure in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the definition: categorical proposition is that which does not resolve into two propositions.
2
Understand the two types of resolution
Note the two possibilities: (a) into two singles, e.g., 'Zaid is standing' becomes 'Zaid' and 'standing'; (b) into one single and one proposition, e.g., 'Zaid, his father is standing' becomes 'Zaid' and 'his father is standing'.
3
Recognize the objection
Some categoricals like 'the rational animal moves by moving its feet' seem to have terms that are not singles, potentially violating the definition.
4
Analyze the response
The term 'single' includes both actual and potential singles; any categorical's terms can be expressed by a single word like 'this' or 'that', unlike conditionals.
Al-Razi supports this explanation.
5
Distinguish from conditionals
Confirm that conditionals always resolve into two propositions and cannot have their terms expressed as singles, reinforcing the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحملية ما لا ينحل إلى قضيتين، بل ينحل إما إلى مفردين كقولك: "زيد" هو قائم" فإنك إذا حذفت الرابطة - أعني "هو" - بقي "زيد" و "قائم" وهما مفردان، وإما إلى مفرد وقضية كما في قولك: "زيد" أبوه قائم، فإذا حللته بقي "زيد" وهو مفرد، و "أبوه قائم" وهو قضية. فإن قلت: قولنا: "الحيوان الناطق ينتقل بنقل قدميه"، وقولنا: "زيد عالم" يُضاده "زيد ليس بعالم، وقولنا: "الشمس طالعة" يلزمه "النهار" "موجود حمليات مع أن أطرافها ليست بمفردات، فانتقض التعريفان طردا و عكسا؟ فنقول: المراد بالمفرد إما المفرد بالفعل أو المفرد بالقوة، وهو الذي يمكن أن يعبر عنه بلفظ مفرد، والأطراف في القضايا المذكورة وإن لم تكن مفردات بالفعل إلا أنه يمكن أن يعبر عنها بالألفاظ مفردة، وأقلها أن يقال: هذا ذاك، أو هو هو، أو الموضوع محمول، بخلاف الشرطيات؛ فإنه لا يمكن أن يعبر عن أطرافها بألفاظ مفردة. كذا قال العلامة الرازي، ولا مساغ لهذا الاعتراض على التقسيم الذي ذكرناه.
The categorical proposition is that which does not resolve into two propositions, but rather resolves either into two singles, like your saying: 'Zaid is standing' - for if you remove the copula, i.e., 'is', there remains 'Zaid' and 'standing', both being singles; or into a single and a proposition, as in your saying: 'Zaid, his father is standing', for if you analyze it, there remains 'Zaid', which is a single, and 'his father is standing', which is a proposition. If you say: Our saying 'the rational animal moves by moving its feet', and our saying 'Zaid is learned' its opposite is 'Zaid is not learned', and our saying 'the sun is risen' entails 'daytime exists' are categorical propositions while their terms are not singles, so the two definitions are violated both in inclusiveness and exclusiveness? We say: By 'single' is meant either actually single or potentially single, which is that it can be expressed by a single word; the terms in the mentioned propositions, though not actually singles, can be expressed by single words, the least of which is to say 'this is that', or 'he is he', or 'the subject is the predicate', unlike conditionals, for it is impossible to express their terms by single words. Thus said the noble al-Razi, and there is no room for this objection to the division we mentioned.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition (al-ḥamliyya) and clarifies its two modes of decomposition. The author begins by stating that a categorical proposition does not resolve into two propositions; instead, it resolves either into two singles (e.g., 'Zaid' and 'standing' after removing the copula) or into one single and one proposition (e.g., 'Zaid' and 'his father is standing'). This definition distinguishes it from conditional propositions, which resolve into two propositions. An objection is then raised: some categorical propositions appear to have terms that are not singles, such as 'the rational animal moves by moving its feet' or 'the sun is risen' entailing 'daytime exists'. The answer is that 'single' here includes both actual and potential singles; the terms can be expressed by single words like 'this' or 'that', unlike conditionals where this is impossible. The author cites al-Razi to support this and concludes that the objection is invalid. This lesson guards against misunderstanding the definition of 'single' as only actual, and emphasizes the difference between categorical and conditional propositions in terms of term resolution.

الحملية
مفرد
رابطة

Key Takeaway
