The Syllogism: Conjunctive and Exceptive
Author defines syllogism and divides it into conjunctive and exceptive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an iqtirani and an istithna'i syllogism and identify which type a given argument belongs to.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a compound of propositions that entails a new statement; its type depends on whether the conclusion is mentioned.

The author defines the syllogism as the core inferential unit in logic. He then divides it based on whether the conclusion or its contradictory appears explicitly in the premises. Understanding this division is essential for recognizing the structure of arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the definition of qiyas
The text says: 'The conjunctive syllogism is from propositions which entail another statement after the acceptance of those propositions.' Focus on the key elements: compound of propositions, entailment, and acceptance of premises.
2
Identify the two types
Look for the phrase 'if the conclusion or its contrary is mentioned' to identify istithna'i. If neither is mentioned, it is iqtirani. This is the single distinguishing criterion.
Istithna'i always includes a conditional premise (e.g., 'If Zayd is human...') and an exception premise.
3
Analyze the examples
The author gives two istithna'i examples (affirmative and negative) and one iqtirani example. For each, locate the premises and the conclusion. Notice how in iqtirani the conclusion is not stated in the premises, while in istithna'i it is.
The iqtirani example is the classic: 'Zayd is human and every human is animal' yields 'Zayd is animal'.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Consider: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Wine is intoxicant. Therefore wine is forbidden.' This is iqtirani because the conclusion is not in the premises. Now consider: 'If wine is intoxicant, it is forbidden. Wine is intoxicant. Therefore it is forbidden.' This is istithna'i because the conclusion is mentioned in the conditional.
The same content can be expressed in both forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضايا وأقسامها القياس الاقتراني من قضايا يلزم عنها قول آخر بعد تسليم تلك القضايا. فإن كان النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورا فيه يسمّى استثنائيا، كقولنا: "إن كان زيد إنسانا كان حيوانا لكنه إنسان" ينتج فهو حيوان. وإن كان زيد حمارا كان ناهقا لكنه ليس بناهق ينتج ليس بحمار. وإن لم تكن النتيجة ونقيضها مذكورا يسمّى اقترانيا كقولك: "زيد إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان" ينتج زيد حيوان.
Propositions and their divisions. The conjunctive syllogism is from propositions which entail another statement after the acceptance of those propositions. If the conclusion or its contrary is mentioned in it, it is called exceptive, like our statement: 'If Zayd is a human, he is an animal, but he is a human' so it yields 'he is an animal.' And if Zayd is a donkey, he is a brayer, but he is not a brayer yields 'he is not a donkey.' And if the conclusion and its contrary are not mentioned, it is called conjunctive, like your statement: 'Zayd is a human and every human is an animal' yields 'Zayd is an animal.'
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) as a compound of propositions which, when accepted, necessarily entail another statement. It then divides the syllogism into two types based on whether the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated within the syllogism. If the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned, it is an exceptive syllogism (istithna'i), which has a conditional premise and an exception premise. The author gives two examples: first, a conditional with affirmative exception, and second, a conditional with negative exception. If neither the conclusion nor its contradictory is mentioned, it is a conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani), composed of categorical premises. The example given is the classic: 'Zayd is a human and every human is an animal' yielding 'Zayd is an animal.' This division is fundamental in Aristotelian logic as transmitted in the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
قياس
اقتراني
استثنائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The iqtirani uses conditional premises.
B) The istithna'i mentions the conclusion or its contrary in the premises.
C) The iqtirani has three figures.
D) The istithna'i always has a true conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental division of syllogisms is based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated in the premises: if yes, it is istithna'i; if no, it is iqtirani.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Then rewrite it as an istithna'i syllogism using a conditional.

The Two Movements of Fikr and the Nature of Hads
Author defines fikr as two movements and contrasts it with hads as instantaneous transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish fikr from hads by identifying the two movements required in fikr and the instantaneous nature of hads.

Core Idea
Fikr is a two-step journey; hads is a single leap.

In this lesson, we explore the author's clear distinction between fikr (thinking) and hads (intuition). Fikr involves two deliberate mental movements, while hads is an instantaneous transition. Understanding this difference is crucial for grasping how the mind arrives at knowledge—whether through gradual reasoning or sudden insight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two movements of fikr
The passage describes fikr as having two movements: first from the sought to the principles (stored meanings), then from the principles back to the sought in a gradual order.
The author emphasizes that both movements are necessary for fikr.
2
Analyze the example of defining 'human'
In the example, the first movement finds 'animal' and 'rational' as appropriate meanings; the second movement arranges them into 'rational animal' to obtain the definition.
This illustrates how the two movements work in practice.
3
Contrast with hads as instantaneous
Hads does not require the two movements; the mind transfers from the sought to the principles and back instantly.
The author explicitly says 'في الحدس ... انتقال الذهن من المطلوب إلى المبادئ دفعة'.
4
Recognize the degrees of hads
The passage categorizes people into those with strong hads (supported by holy power), weak hads, and no hads (extreme dullness).
This shows that hads varies among individuals and can be enhanced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان وما يتعلق به الفرق بين الحدس والفكر أن يكون هناك حركة فكرية والفرق بين الحدس والفكر أنه لا بد في الفكر من الحركتين للنفس، بخلاف الحدس؛ فإن الذهن بعد ما حصل له المطلوب بوجه ما يتحرك في المعاني المخزونة والمبادئ المكنونة طالبا لما يكون لها تناسب بالمطلوب حتى يجد معلومات مناسبة له وههنا تمّ الحركة الأولى ثم يرجع قهقهرى، ويتحرك ثانيا مرتبا لتلك المعلومات المخزونة التي وجدها ترتيبا تدريجيا حتى وصل إلى المطلوب، وتم الحركة الثانية، فمجموع هاتين الحركتين يسمّى بالفكر، مثلا: إذا كنت تصورت الإنسان بوجه من الوجوه كالكاتب والضاحك مثلا، ثم صرت طالبا لماهية الإنسان؛ فحركت ذهنك نحو المعاني التي عندك مخزونة، فوجدت الحيوان والناطق مناسبا لمطلوبك، فتمّ الحركة الأولى، ومبدؤه المطلوب المعلوم من وجه ومنتهاه الحيوان والناطق، ثم ترتب الحيوان والناطق بأن تقدّم الحيوان الذي هو الجنس من على الناطق الذي هو الفصل وقلت الحيوان الناطق وههنا انقطع الحركة الثانية، وحصل المطلوب. وأما الحدس ففيه انتقال الذهن من المطلوب إلى المبادئ دفعة، ومنها إلى المطلوب كذلك. وأكثر ما يكون الحدس عقيب الشوق والتعب، وقد تكون بدونها. والناس مختلفون في الحدس؛ فمنهم من هو قوي الحدس كثيره يحصل من المطالب أكثرها بالحدس كالمؤيد بالقوة القدسية كالحكماء والأولياء والأنبياء. ومنهم من هو قليل الحدس ضعيفه. ومنهم من لا حدس له كالمنتهي في البلادة.
Demonstration and what pertains to it: The distinction between intuition and thinking is that there is a mental movement. The distinction between intuition and thinking is that in thinking there must be two movements of the soul, unlike intuition. For the mind, after it has obtained the sought in some respect, moves among stored meanings and hidden principles seeking what has a correspondence to the sought until it finds appropriate information. Here the first movement is completed. Then it returns backwards and moves a second time, arranging those stored information that it found in a gradual order until it reaches the sought, and the second movement is completed. So the sum of these two movements is called thinking. For example: If you have conceived of 'human' in some respect, such as 'writer' or 'laughing' for example, then you become a seeker of the essence of human, so you move your mind towards the meanings stored with you, and you find 'animal' and 'rational' appropriate for your sought. So the first movement is completed, its beginning is the sought known in some respect and its end is 'animal' and 'rational'. Then you arrange 'animal' and 'rational' by presenting 'animal' which is the genus over 'rational' which is the differentia, and you say 'rational animal', and here the second movement is cut off, and the sought is obtained. As for intuition, in it the mind transfers from the sought to the principles instantly, and from them to the sought likewise. And most often intuition occurs after longing and exhaustion, and it may occur without them. People differ in intuition: among them is one who has strong intuition, many of his sought things are obtained by intuition, like one supported by holy power, such as the philosophers, saints, and prophets. Among them is one who has little intuition, weak in it. Among them is one who has no intuition, like one extreme in stupidity.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between fikr (thinking) and hads (intuition). The author begins by stating that fikr necessarily involves two movements of the soul: the first movement goes from the sought object (al-matlab) to the principles (al-mabadi') that are stored in the mind; the second movement goes from those principles back to the sought object in a gradual arrangement. The sum of these two movements is called fikr. An example is given: one wants to know the essence (mahiyya) of 'human'. Initially, one has a vague conception of human (e.g., as a writer or laughing). The first movement seeks stored meanings and finds 'animal' and 'rational' as suitable. The second movement arranges them by placing 'animal' as genus and 'rational' as differentia, yielding the definition 'rational animal', and thus the sought is attained. In contrast, hads is an instantaneous transfer from the sought to the principles and back, without the two movements. Hads often occurs after intense longing and effort, but can also occur spontaneously. People vary in hads: some have strong intuition, like philosophers, saints, and prophets supported by holy power; some have weak intuition; and some have no intuition at all, reaching the extreme of dullness. The commentary follows the author's order: first the definition of fikr with its two movements, then the example, then the definition of hads, then the degrees of hads among people. The example from the Islamic sciences is the search for the definition of human, a standard topic in logic and philosophy.

Key Terms
الفكر
الحدس
الحركة الأولى
الحركة الثانية
القوة القدسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fikr uses stored meanings, hads does not.
B) Fikr involves two movements, hads involves one movement.
C) Fikr requires two sequential movements, hads is instantaneous.
D) Fikr is for non-prophets, hads only for prophets.

Key Takeaway
Fikr is a gradual reasoning process with two movements, while hads is an immediate insight without sequential steps.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classification of Perceptions: External and Internal Senses
Classifies perceptions into external (five senses) and internal (five internal senses) categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the five external senses from the five internal senses, and identify the function and anatomical location of each internal sense as described in this commentary.

Core Idea
Perceptions include not only external senses but also internal faculties and self-awareness, forming a complete epistemological framework.

In Islamic logic, the foundation of certain knowledge begins with perceptions (mushahadat). This passage from the commentary expands the basic definition by detailing the five external and five internal senses, giving both physiological and functional descriptions. Understanding this classification is crucial for grasping how Islamic scholars structured empirical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two main categories
Identify that perceptions are divided into external senses (hissiyyat) and internal senses (batinah). Note the third type, wijdaniyyat (self-awareness), which is also included.
2
Analyze the external senses
For each of the five external senses (sight, hearing, smell, taste, touch), note the specific organ and the qualities perceived, as described in the text.
3
Understand the internal senses
For each of the five internal senses (common sense, imagination, estimation, memory), note its function and its locus in the brain according to the passage.
4
Recognize the third type: wijdaniyyat
Identify that wijdaniyyat includes internal awareness and self-knowledge, distinct from both external and internal senses. This is key for arguments about self-awareness.

المشاهدات وأقسامها ورابعها المشاهدات وهي قضايا يحكم فيها بواسطة المشاهدة والإحساس. وهي تنقسم إلى قسمين الأول ما شوهد بإحدى الحواس الظاهرة، وهي خمس: الباصرة والسامعة والشامة والذائقة واللامسة، ويسمى هذا القسم بالحسيات. والثاني: ما أدرك بالمدركات من الحواس الباطنة التي هي أيضا خمس: الحس المشترك: المدرك للصور. والخيال: التي هي خزانة له. والوهم: المدرك للمعاني الشخصية والجزئية. والحافظة: التي هي خزانة للمعاني الجزئية. المشاهدات: إلخ اعلم أن المشاهدات ثلاثة أقسام: الأول: ما نجده بحواسنا الظاهرة كالحكم بأن الشمس مشرقة، والنار محرقة. الثاني: ما نجده بحواسنا الباطنة كالحكم بأن لنا جوعا وعطشا. الثالث: ما نجده بنفوسنا من غير دخل للآلات وهي كشعورنا بذواتنا، وبأفعال ذواتنا. والأخيران يسميان وجدانيات، هذا إذا لم يكن مدركات العقل الصرف مندرجة في القسم الثاني. وإن أريد بالحس الباطن قوة سوى الحس الظاهر؛ فيدخل مدركات العقل الصرف أيضا في هذا القسم. وهي خمس إلخ الباصرة: هي قوة مودعة في العصبتين المجوّفتين اللتين تتلاقيان ثم تفترقان ويتناهيان إلى العين، يدرك بها الأضواء والألوان والأشكال. والسامعة: قوة مودعة في العصب المفروش في مقعر الصماخ، يدرك بها الأصوات بطريق وصول الهواء المتكيف بكيفية الصوت إليها. والشامة: قوة مودعة في الزائدتين النائبتين في مقدّم الدماغ الشبيهتين بحلمي الثدي يدرك بها الروائح بطريق وصول الهواء المتكيف بكيفية ذي الرائحة إلى الخيشوم. والذائقة: قوة منبتة في العصب المفروش على جرم اللسان، يدرك بها الطعوم لمخالطة الرطوبة اللعابية التي في الفم بالمطعوم، ووصولها إلى العصب. واللامسة: قوة سارية بواسطة الأعصاب في جميع البدن، بها يدرك الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة والخشونة والملاسة والصلابة واللين وغيرها. الحس المشترك إلخ: هي القوة التي ترسم فيها صور الجزئيات المحسوسة. ومحله مقدم التجويف الأول من الدماغ. والخيال إلخ: هي قوة تحفظ ما يدركه الحس المشترك من صور المحسوسات بعد غيبوبة المادة بحيث يشاهدها الحس المشترك كلما التفت إليها فهي خزانة. والوهم إلخ: هي قوة مرتبة في أول التجويف الآخر من الدماغ، وهو المدرك للمعاني الجزئية المتعلقة بالمحسوسات كالعداوة الجزئية التي تدركها الشاة الذئب فيهرب عنه. والحافظة إلخ: هي قوة محلها آخر التجويف الآخر من الدماغ، وهي للوهم كالخيال للحس المشترك.
Perceptions and their divisions: The fourth [type of premises] is perceptions (mushahadat), which are propositions judged by means of observation and sensation. They are divided into two parts: first, what is observed by one of the external senses, which are five: sight, hearing, smell, taste, and touch – this part is called sensibles (hissiyyat). Second, what is apprehended by the perceptions from the internal senses, which are also five: the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak): the perceiver of forms; the imagination (al-khayal): which is the treasury for it; the estimation (al-wahm): the perceiver of individual and particular meanings; the memory (al-hafiza): which is the treasury for particular meanings. 'Perceptions etc.': Know that perceptions are three types: first, what we find by our external senses, like the judgment that the sun is shining and fire is burning; second, what we find by our internal senses, like the judgment that we have hunger and thirst; third, what we find by ourselves without instruments, like our awareness of our own selves and our own actions. The last two are called 'found things' (wijdaniyyat), this is if the perceptions of pure intellect are not included in the second type. If by internal sense is meant a power other than the external sense, then the perceptions of pure intellect also enter this type. 'They are five etc.': The sight (al-basira) is a power lodged in the two hollow nerves that meet then separate and end at the eye; by it lights, colors, and shapes are perceived. The hearing (al-sami'a) is a power lodged in the nerve spread in the concavity of the ear; by it sounds are perceived through the arrival of air modified by the quality of the sound. The smell (al-shamma) is a power lodged in the two protrusions in the front of the brain resembling the nipples of the breast; by it odors are perceived through the arrival of air modified by the quality of the odorous substance to the nasal cavity. The taste (al-dha'iqa) is a power growing in the nerve spread on the body of the tongue; by it tastes are perceived through the mixing of the salivary moisture in the mouth with the tasted substance and its arrival at the nerve. The touch (al-lamisa) is a power flowing through nerves in the entire body; by it heat, cold, moisture, dryness, roughness, smoothness, hardness, softness, etc. are perceived. 'The common sense etc.' is the power in which forms of sensed particulars are delineated; its locus is the front of the first ventricle of the brain. 'The imagination etc.' is a power that preserves what the common sense perceives of the forms of sensibles after the disappearance of the matter, such that the common sense perceives them whenever it turns to them; it is a treasury. 'The estimation etc.' is a power located in the beginning of the other ventricle of the brain; it perceives the particular meanings related to sensibles, like the particular enmity that the sheep perceives in the wolf and flees from it. 'The memory etc.' is a power whose locus is the end of the other ventricle of the brain; it is to the estimation what the imagination is to the common sense.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a classificatory and definitional commentary on the fourth type of premises (mushahadat). It first defines perceptions as propositions based on observation and sensation. The author divides them into two main parts: external senses (five: sight, hearing, smell, taste, touch) and internal senses (five: common sense, imagination, estimation, memory, and one more, but the text lists only four; the missing one is perhaps the imaginative faculty (mutakhayyila) or the retentive imagination, but the text goes on to describe three types of perceptions: external, internal, and a third type called 'wijdaniyyat' which are self-awareness without instruments. The author then describes each external sense anatomically and functionally, and each internal sense with its locus in the brain: common sense (front of first ventricle) perceives forms; imagination (stores forms after disappearance of matter); estimation (perceives particular meanings like enmity); memory (stores those meanings). The passage emphasizes the distinction between sensory and intellectual perceptions.

Key Terms
المشاهدات
الحس المشترك
الوهم
الوجدانيات

Islamic epistemological classification of perceptions includes not only the five external senses but also five internal faculties and self-awareness, each with a defined function and brain locus.

Definition of the Poetic Syllogism
Author defines the poetic syllogism and its preference for false imaginings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the poetic syllogism and explain why false imaginative propositions are preferred over true primary ones.

Core Idea
The poetic syllogism moves the soul through beautiful lies, not through truth.

The poetic syllogism is one of the five arts of logic, but unlike demonstrative syllogisms, it does not aim at certainty. Instead, it aims at emotional impact using metaphors and similes. In this lesson, we explore its definition and the surprising principle that false propositions are considered excellent in this art.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the poetic syllogism
The author states its purpose: to produce a profound effect on the soul causing joy or grief.
2
Note the types of propositions it uses
It avoids true primary propositions (al-awwaliyat al-sadiqa) and prefers false imaginative ones (al-mukhayyilat al-kadhiba).
3
Examine the supporting quote
The Persian line from al-Kunjawi reinforces that the falsest poetry is the most beautiful.
Quote: 'dar shiʿr hīch wa dar fan ū chūn akdhab ūst ahsan ū'
4
Formulate the definition
The poetic syllogism is a syllogism composed of imaginative premises that are false but emotionally effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غرض هذه الصناعة القياس الشعري استعارات بديعة رائقة وتشبيهات أنيقة فائقة بحيث يؤثر في النفس تأثيرا عجيبا، ويورث فرحا أو يوجب ترحا، ومن ثَم لا يجوز فيه استعمال الأوليات الصادقة، ويستحسن استعمال المخيّلات الكاذبة، كما قال العارف الكنجوي مخاطبا بولده فلذة كبده: در شعر هیچ و در فن او چوں اکذب اوست احسن او وكقول القائل يصف الخمر: لها البدر كأس وهي شمس يديرها هلال وكم يبدو إذا مزجت نجم وقال الشاعر: لا تعجبوا من بلى غلالته قد زرّ أزراره على القمر فشبه المحبوب بالقمر وقال: "لا" تعجبوا من انشقاق غلالته؛ لأنه قمر زرّ عليه الغلالة وكل قمر كذلك فغلالته تنشق"، ينتج "غلالة المحبوب تنشق". وقد ينتج اجتماع النقيضين نحو: "أنا مضمر الحوائج باللسان مظهرها بالمدامع، وكل مضمر الحوائج صامت وكل مظهرها متكلم" ينتج "أنا صامت متكلم". ولا يشترط الوزن في الشعر عند أرباب الميزان، نعم، يفيده حسنا. والكلام الشعري إذا أنشد بصوت طيب ازداد تأثيره في النفوس، حتى ربما يزيل فرط البهجة العمائم عن الرؤوس. والأوائل من الحكماء اليونانيين كانوا أحرص الناس على الشعر.
The purpose of this art is the poetic syllogism: (it consists of) brilliant, radiant metaphors and exquisite, superior similes, such that it affects the soul with an amazing effect, causing joy or grief. Therefore, true primary propositions cannot be used in it, and false imaginative propositions are considered good, as the mystic al-Kunjawi said addressing his son, the piece of his liver: 'In poetry and its art, just as its falsest is its most beautiful.' And like the saying of one describing wine: 'The full moon is its cup, and it is a sun, a crescent moon rotates it—how many stars appear when it is mixed?' And the poet said: 'Do not wonder at the fraying of his shirt; he has buttoned its buttons over the moon.' So he likened the beloved to the moon and said: 'Do not wonder at the tearing of his shirt; because he is a moon over whom the shirt is buttoned, and every moon is like that, so its shirt tears.' The result is 'the beloved's shirt tears.' And sometimes it produces the conjunction of opposites, like: 'I am one who hides needs with my tongue and reveals them with tears, and everyone who hides needs is silent, and everyone who reveals them is speaking.' The result is 'I am silent and speaking.' And weight (meter) is not a condition in poetry according to the masters of the standard, but it does benefit beauty. And poetic speech, when recited with a pleasant voice, increases its effect on souls, so much so that it might remove turbans from heads due to extreme joy. And the early Greek philosophers were the most eager of people for poetry.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the poetic syllogism (al-qiyas al-shiʿri) as the art of using striking metaphors (istiʿarat badiʿa) and elegant similes (tashbihat anīqa) to produce a profound emotional effect—joy or sorrow. The author explains that truth (awwaliyat sadiqa) is not used; instead, imaginative falsehoods (mukhayyilat kadhiba) are preferred, citing a Persian saying that the best poetry is the most false. Two examples are given: one describing wine using celestial imagery (moon, sun, crescent, stars) and another where the beloved is likened to the moon, leading to a syllogistic conclusion that the beloved's shirt tears, as every moon's shirt tears. A second example shows the conjunction of opposites: someone hiding needs while revealing them through tears, resulting in the contradiction of being both silent and speaking. The passage then clarifies that meter (wazn) is not essential for poetry according to logicians, though it enhances beauty, and recitation with a pleasant voice amplifies its impact. The early Greek philosophers are noted as great lovers of poetry.

القياس الشعري
المخيّلات

A) True primary propositions
B) False imaginative propositions

D) Rational propositions

Key Takeaway
The poetic syllogism prioritizes emotional effect over truth, using false imaginative premises.

Analyzing the Moon Simile as a Poetic Syllogism
Example of the poetic syllogism using the moon simile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the poetic syllogism from the moon simile and identify its premises and conclusion.

Core Idea
In poetry, a simile can serve as a minor premise in a syllogism that yields an emotionally resonant conclusion.

The author provides a concrete example of a poetic syllogism: a poet says, 'Do not wonder at the tearing of his shirt; because he is a moon over whom the shirt is buttoned, and every moon's shirt tears.' This is not a real syllogism but a poetic one, where the comparison is treated as identity. In this lesson, we dissect this example.

Guided Path
1
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion is 'the beloved's shirt tears' (inṭāj: ghilālat al-maḥbūb tanshaq).
2
Identify the premises
The argument proceeds: 'Every moon's shirt tears' (implicit universal premise) and 'The beloved is a moon' (the simile).
3
Recognize the logical form
This resembles a categorical syllogism in Barbara mood: All M have property P, The beloved is M, therefore the beloved has P.
The form is valid in shape, but the premises are not required to be true.
4
Note the poetic license
The premise 'Every moon's shirt tears' is not literally true but is accepted as an imaginative premise for the poem's sake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غرض هذه الصناعة القياس الشعري استعارات بديعة رائقة وتشبيهات أنيقة فائقة بحيث يؤثر في النفس تأثيرا عجيبا، ويورث فرحا أو يوجب ترحا، ومن ثَم لا يجوز فيه استعمال الأوليات الصادقة، ويستحسن استعمال المخيّلات الكاذبة، كما قال العارف الكنجوي مخاطبا بولده فلذة كبده: در شعر هیچ و در فن او چوں اکذب اوست احسن او وكقول القائل يصف الخمر: لها البدر كأس وهي شمس يديرها هلال وكم يبدو إذا مزجت نجم وقال الشاعر: لا تعجبوا من بلى غلالته قد زرّ أزراره على القمر فشبه المحبوب بالقمر وقال: "لا" تعجبوا من انشقاق غلالته؛ لأنه قمر زرّ عليه الغلالة وكل قمر كذلك فغلالته تنشق"، ينتج "غلالة المحبوب تنشق". وقد ينتج اجتماع النقيضين نحو: "أنا مضمر الحوائج باللسان مظهرها بالمدامع، وكل مضمر الحوائج صامت وكل مظهرها متكلم" ينتج "أنا صامت متكلم". ولا يشترط الوزن في الشعر عند أرباب الميزان، نعم، يفيده حسنا. والكلام الشعري إذا أنشد بصوت طيب ازداد تأثيره في النفوس، حتى ربما يزيل فرط البهجة العمائم عن الرؤوس. والأوائل من الحكماء اليونانيين كانوا أحرص الناس على الشعر.
The purpose of this art is the poetic syllogism: (it consists of) brilliant, radiant metaphors and exquisite, superior similes, such that it affects the soul with an amazing effect, causing joy or grief. Therefore, true primary propositions cannot be used in it, and false imaginative propositions are considered good, as the mystic al-Kunjawi said addressing his son, the piece of his liver: 'In poetry and its art, just as its falsest is its most beautiful.' And like the saying of one describing wine: 'The full moon is its cup, and it is a sun, a crescent moon rotates it—how many stars appear when it is mixed?' And the poet said: 'Do not wonder at the fraying of his shirt; he has buttoned its buttons over the moon.' So he likened the beloved to the moon and said: 'Do not wonder at the tearing of his shirt; because he is a moon over whom the shirt is buttoned, and every moon is like that, so its shirt tears.' The result is 'the beloved's shirt tears.' And sometimes it produces the conjunction of opposites, like: 'I am one who hides needs with my tongue and reveals them with tears, and everyone who hides needs is silent, and everyone who reveals them is speaking.' The result is 'I am silent and speaking.' And weight (meter) is not a condition in poetry according to the masters of the standard, but it does benefit beauty. And poetic speech, when recited with a pleasant voice, increases its effect on souls, so much so that it might remove turbans from heads due to extreme joy. And the early Greek philosophers were the most eager of people for poetry.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the poetic syllogism (al-qiyas al-shiʿri) as the art of using striking metaphors (istiʿarat badiʿa) and elegant similes (tashbihat anīqa) to produce a profound emotional effect—joy or sorrow. The author explains that truth (awwaliyat sadiqa) is not used; instead, imaginative falsehoods (mukhayyilat kadhiba) are preferred, citing a Persian saying that the best poetry is the most false. Two examples are given: one describing wine using celestial imagery (moon, sun, crescent, stars) and another where the beloved is likened to the moon, leading to a syllogistic conclusion that the beloved's shirt tears, as every moon's shirt tears. A second example shows the conjunction of opposites: someone hiding needs while revealing them through tears, resulting in the contradiction of being both silent and speaking. The passage then clarifies that meter (wazn) is not essential for poetry according to logicians, though it enhances beauty, and recitation with a pleasant voice amplifies its impact. The early Greek philosophers are noted as great lovers of poetry.

Key Terms
القياس الشعري
المخيّلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the moon simile example, what is the middle term?
A) The beloved
B) The shirt
C) Moon
D) Tears

Key Takeaway
A poetic syllogism can have a valid form even if its premises are false, as long as they are emotionally effective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a famous Islamic poetic line, e.g., 'The stars are the tears of the sky' (al-Maʿarrī). Reconstruct it as a poetic syllogism: identify the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion.
?
Check whether the syllogism is valid in form and explain why it is acceptable in poetry.

Conjunction of Opposites and Conditions of Poetry
Example of conjunction of opposites and discussion of meter and voice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conjunction of opposites in a poetic syllogism and explain the logicians' view on meter and voice.

Core Idea
Poetry can deliberately produce logical contradictions to enhance emotional impact, and meter is not essential but beneficial.

The author presents another example: a person who hides needs with his tongue but reveals them with tears, resulting in the contradictory conclusion that he is both silent and speaking. This exemplifies how poetry can embrace opposites. The passage also discusses the role of meter and the effect of recitation with a pleasant voice.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the second example
The premises: 'Everyone who hides needs is silent' and 'Everyone who reveals needs is speaking.' The speaker does both, so he is both silent and speaking.
2
Recognize the conjunction of opposites
The conclusion is a logical contradiction (silent and speaking), which is normally invalid but acceptable in poetry.
3
Examine the condition of meter
The author says meter is not a requirement for poetry according to logicians (arbāb al-mīzān), though it enhances beauty.
4
Consider the effect of recitation
Reciting with a pleasant voice increases emotional impact, even to the point of making listeners remove their turbans in joy.

The Subject of a Science: Definition and Status
Author defines the subject of a science and debates whether it is part of the science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of any science (subject, principles, problems) and explain whether the subject is part of the science.

Core Idea
The subject of a science is its unifying foundation, but it is not an independent part of that science.

This concluding passage explains what every science must have: a subject, principles, and problems. The subject is defined and its relationship to the science is clarified through a debate by al-Shirazi. Understanding this structure helps you see how a science is organized and what can be investigated within it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components of a science
Locate the three things that every science has: subject (mawḍūʿ), principles (mabādiʾ), and problems (masāʾil). The text lists them at the beginning.
These are the structural pillars of any science.
2
Define the subject of a science
Find the definition: the subject is that about which the science investigates its accidents and essential concomitants. Note the examples: human body for medicine, word/speech for grammar, continuous quantity for geometry, conceptual and propositional known for logic.
The definition shows what the science focuses on.
3
Analyze al-Shirazi's debate on whether the subject is part of the science
The text presents two interpretations: If 'subject' means assent to its subjecthood, it is a preliminary, not a part. If it means conception of the subject, it is a principle, not an independent part. Understand the distinction.
This clarifies a common confusion: the subject is not itself a part of the science's content.
4
Explain the rule about essential attributes (dhātiyyāt)
The essential attributes (genus and differentia) of the subject must be taken as givens. One cannot prove them within the science. This ensures the science has a firm foundation.
This prevents circularity in scientific reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة
لكل علم ثلاث أمور: أحدها الموضوع، وهو ما يبحث في العلم عن عوارضه ولواحقه الذاتية كبدن الإنسان لعلم الطب والكلمة والكلام لعلم النحو، والمقدار المتصل لعلم الهندسة والمعلوم التصوري والمعلوم التصديقي لصناعتي هذه.
أحدها: الموضوع قال العلامة الشيرازي في كون الموضوع جزء من العلم على حدة نظر؛ لأنه إن أريد به التصديق بالموضوعية فهو ليس من أجزاء العلوم؛ لعدم توقف العلم عليه، بل هو من مقدمات الشروع منه على ما مرّ. وإن أريد به تصور الموضوع فهو من المبادئ، وليس جزء بالاستقلال واعلم أن القدر الضروري هو كون ذاتيات موضوع العلم من المسلمات في ذلك العلم، حتى أنه يجب الاجتناب عن جعل جنس موضوع العلم وفصله محمولا في ذلك العلم. وكذا يمتنع إيراد مبادئ ذاتياته في المسائل.
والمعلوم التصوري إلخ بهذا ظهر أنه يجوز أن يكون موضوع العلم أمورا متعددة، إلا أنه لا بد من اشتراكه في أمر واحد يلاحظه في سائر مباحث العلم، كموضوعات هذا الفن؛ فإنها مشتركة في الإيصال إلى مطلوب مجهول. والإيجاز أن يكون العلوم المتفرقة علما واحدا.
Conclusion: Every science has three things: one of them is the subject, which is that about which the science investigates its accidents and essential concomitants, like the human body for medicine, the word and speech for grammar, continuous quantity for geometry, and the conceptual known and the propositional known for these two arts. The first: the subject—al-Shirazi said concerning the subject being a part of the science as a separate entity: there is consideration; because if what is meant by it is the assent to subjectivity, then it is not from the parts of the sciences because the science does not depend on it, rather it is among the preliminaries of starting, as has passed. And if what is meant by it is the conception of the subject, then it is from the principles, and it is not an independent part. And know that the necessary extent is that the essentials of the subject of a science are among the givens in that science, such that one must avoid making the genus of the subject and its differentia predicated in that science. Likewise, it is forbidden to introduce the principles of its essentials into the problems. 'The conceptual known, etc.': By this it becomes clear that it is permissible for the subject of a science to be multiple things, but they must share in a single thing that is observed throughout the investigations of the science, like the subjects of this art; they share in leading to an unknown sought. In brief, this makes scattered sciences a single science.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the subject of a science. The author begins the concluding chapter (khātima) by stating that every science has three aspects: subject (mawḍūʿ), principles (mabādiʾ), and problems (masāʾil). The subject is defined as that about which the science investigates its accidents and essential concomitants, with examples from medicine, grammar, geometry, and logic. The commentary then delves into the debate about whether the subject is part of the science itself. Citing al-Shirazi, it distinguishes two interpretations: if 'subject' means assent to its subjecthood, it is not part of the science but a preliminary; if it means conception of the subject, it is among the principles, not an independent part. The passage further asserts that the essential attributes (dhātiyyāt) of the subject must be taken as givens within the science, so their genus and differentia cannot be proven within it. Finally, it notes that the subject can be multiple things as long as they share a single unifying aspect, giving logic's diverse topics unified by leading to the unknown.

Key Terms
موضوع
ذاتيات
مبادئ
عوارض ذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is an independent part of the science.
B) It is the same as the principles.
C) Its essential attributes are taken as givens within the science.
D) It must be a single thing only.

Key Takeaway
The subject of a science is that which the science studies, but its essential attributes are presupposed, not proven, within that science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The First Seven Headings of Book Prefaces
Author enumerates the first seven of eight preface headings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the first seven of the eight standard preface headings and their roles in orienting the reader.

Core Idea
The first seven headings establish purpose, credibility, and curricular placement.

Islamic scholars developed a standardized preface to guide readers. The first seven headings—aim, benefit, naming, author, rank, genus, and division—provide essential context about the book's goals, author, and structure. Understanding them helps you quickly assess any scholarly work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven headings
From the Arabic text, locate the first seven headings: الغرض (aim), المنفعة (benefit), التسمية (naming), المؤلف (author), المرتبة (rank), من أي علم (genus), القسمة (division).
The eighth heading (modes of instruction) is treated separately.
2
State the purpose of each heading
For each heading, note why it is included: aim prevents aimlessness, benefit motivates, naming summarizes, author reassures, rank shows precedence, genus fits the discipline, division outlines structure.
The commentary emphasizes that knowing the author 'sets the learner's heart at ease'.
3
Explain the commentary expansions
The commentary adds details: the rank of logic is after ethics and geometry; the science belongs to rational or transmitted types; division includes both science and book sections.
These expansions show how the headings are applied in practice.
4
Connect to a real preface
Consider how a book like al-Ghazali's Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din uses these headings: the aim is to revive religious sciences, the author's credentials are given, and the division into four quarters is stated.
This helps you recognize the headings in any classical text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الرؤوس الثمانية: اعلم أن القدماء كانوا يذكرون في مبادئ الكتب أشياء ثمانية، ويسمونها "الرؤوس الثمانية، أحدها الغرض، أعني العلة الغائية؛ لئلا يكون الناظر عابثا. وثانيها المنفعة؛ لتسهل عليه المشقة في تحصيله. وثالثها: التسمية، أعني عنوان العلم؛ ليكون عند الناظر إجمال ما يفصله الغرض.
ورابعها المؤلّف؛ ليسكن قلب المتعلم وخامسها أنه في أي مرتبة هو؛ ليعلم على أيّ علم يجب تقديمه، وعن أيّ علم يجب تأخيره. وسادسها: من أي علم هو؛ ليطلب ما يليق به وسابعها القسمة وهو أبواب العلم والكتاب. وثامنها: أنحاء التعليم وهي التقسيم والتحليل والتحديد والبرهان؛ ليعرف أن الكتاب مشتمل على كلها أو بعضها.
ورابعها المؤلف إلخ: بكسر اللام أي تعيين المؤلّف ومعرفته ليسكن حال المتعلم، ويطمئن قلبه في قبول كلامه بالاعتماد عليه؛ لأن معرفة حال الأقوال بمراتب الرجال. في أي مرتبة هو إلخ كما يقال إن مرتبة المنطق أن يشتغل به بعد تهذيب الأخلاق، وتقويم الفكر ببعض الهندسيات. أيّ علم هو إلخ من أي جنس من أجناس العلوم العقلية أو النقلية، أو الفرعية، أو الأصلية. القسمة إلخ أي قسمة العلم والكتاب. فالأول أي قسمة العلم كما يقال: أبواب المنطق تسعة: الأول الكليات الخمس، الثاني التعريفات الثالث القياس الرابع القضايا وأخواته الخامس البرهان السادس الجدل، السابع الخطابة، الثامن المغالطة، التاسع الشعر. وبعضهم عد بحث الألفاظ بابا آخر؛ فصار أبواب المنطق عشرة كاملة. والثاني أي قسمة الكتاب، فتقسيمه بالأبواب والفصول شائع لا يخلو عنه كتاب كما ترى. وهي التقسيم إلخ التقسيم عبارة عن التكثر من فوق إلى أسفل كتقسيم الجنس إلى الأنواع، والنوع إلى الأصناف، والذاتي إلى الجنس والنوع والفصل والعرض إلى الخاصة والعرض العام والتحليل هو التكثير من السفل إلى فوق والتحديد فعل الحد، وهو يدل على الشيء بما به قوامه دلالة مفصلة والبرهان طريق موثوق به، موصل إلى الوقوف على الحق هذا. وإن شئت مزيد تفصيل في أنحاء التعليم فعليك النظر في كتب الفن.
Section on the eight headings: Know that the ancients used to mention eight things in the introductions of books, and they called them 'the eight headings'. First: the aim (gharad), meaning the final cause, so that the inquirer is not aimless. Second: the benefit (manfa'a), so that the difficulty of obtaining it becomes easy for him. Third: the naming (tasmiya), meaning the title of the science, so that the inquirer has a summary of what the aim details. Fourth: the author (mu'allaf), to calm the heart of the learner. Fifth: what rank it is in, so that he knows which science should precede and which should follow. Sixth: which science it is from, so that he seeks what is suitable for it. Seventh: the division (qisma), which are the sections of the science and the book. Eighth: the modes of instruction (anwā' al-ta'līm), which are division, analysis, definition, and demonstration, so that he knows whether the book contains all or some of them. And the fourth: by breaking the lam, i.e., specifying the author and knowing him so that the learner's condition calms and his heart is at ease in accepting his words by relying on him, because knowing the states of statements is by the ranks of men. 'In what rank it is' etc., as it is said that the rank of logic is that one should occupy himself with it after refining morals and correcting thought with some geometrical matters. 'Which science it is from' etc., from which genus of the genera of sciences: rational or transmitted, subsidiary or original. 'The division' etc., i.e., the division of the science and the book. The first, i.e., the division of the science as it is said: The sections of logic are nine: first the five universals, second definitions, third syllogism, fourth propositions and their sisters, fifth demonstration, sixth dialectic, seventh rhetoric, eighth sophistry, ninth poetry. And some counted the discussion of terms as another section, so the sections of logic became a complete ten. The second, i.e., the division of the book, so its division into chapters and sections is widespread and no book is without it as you see. And they are 'the division' etc. Division is the multiplication from top to bottom, like dividing genus into species, species into varieties, essential into genus, species, difference, accident into property and general accident. Analysis is the multiplication from bottom to top. Definition (hadd) is the act of defining, which indicates something by what constitutes it in detailed indication. Demonstration is a trustworthy method leading to the discovery of truth. This. And if you want more detail on the modes of instruction, then you must look into the books of the art.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the eight headings (al-ru'us al-thamaniya) that the ancients used to include in the introductions of their books. The commentator first enumerates the eight headings as presented by the author (musannif): (1) the aim (gharad), i.e., the final cause, so that the reader is not aimless; (2) the benefit (manfa'a), to make the effort worthwhile; (3) the naming (tasmiya), i.e., the title of the science, giving the reader a summary of what the aim details; (4) the author (mu'allaf), to reassure the learner; (5) the rank (martaba), so the learner knows which science should precede or follow; (6) which genus of science it belongs to (e.g., rational, transmitted, subsidiary, original); (7) the division (qisma), i.e., the sections of the science and the book; and (8) the modes of instruction (anwā' al-ta'līm), which are four: division (taqsīm, from top to bottom), analysis (taḥlīl, from bottom to top), definition (taḥdīd, indicating essence in detail), and demonstration (burhān, trustworthy method to reach truth). The commentary then expands on each: for example, specifying the author sets the learner's heart at ease; the rank of logic is after moral refinement and some geometry; the division of the science of logic yields nine or ten sections (the nine classical topics plus optionally the discussion of terms); division is breaking the genus into species, analysis the reverse; definition gives detailed essential description; demonstration is a reliable method. The commentator concludes that further detail can be found in specialized works. Throughout, examples from the Islamic sciences (like the sections of logic) are used.

The first seven headings provide a comprehensive methodological introduction, covering purpose, credibility, and structure of a scholarly work.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
5 min
The Four Modes of Instruction (أنحاء التعليم)
Author explains the eighth heading: four modes of instruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four modes of instruction—division, analysis, definition, and demonstration—and explain how they structure a book.

Core Idea
Division, analysis, definition, and demonstration are the pedagogical tools that shape how knowledge is presented.

The eighth heading of the book preface is the modes of instruction (أنحاء التعليم). These four methods—division (تقسيم), analysis (تحليل), definition (تحديد), and demonstration (برهان)—are the backbone of Islamic scholarly writing. Understanding them helps you follow the author's teaching strategy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four modes
From the Arabic text, find the four modes: التقسيم (division), التحليل (analysis), التحديد (definition), البرهان (demonstration).
The commentary calls them 'the modes of instruction'.
2
Understand division and analysis
Division (taqsīm) breaks from top to bottom: genus into species, species into varieties. Analysis (taḥlīl) moves from bottom to top: from particulars to universals.
Example: dividing 'animal' into rational and non-rational; analyzing 'human' back to animal plus rational.
3
Understand definition and demonstration
Definition (taḥdīd) indicates the essence in detailed indication. Demonstration (burhān) is a trustworthy method leading to truth.
In logic, definition gives the genus and differentia; demonstration proves a proposition with certainty.
4
Apply to a logic textbook
Consider how a standard logic work uses these modes: it divides logic into parts (division), analyzes concepts from simple to complex (analysis), defines terms like 'syllogism' (definition), and proves validity of moods (demonstration).
This is exactly what the commentator implies when he says the modes help know if the book contains all or some of them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الرؤوس الثمانية: اعلم أن القدماء كانوا يذكرون في مبادئ الكتب أشياء ثمانية، ويسمونها "الرؤوس الثمانية، أحدها الغرض، أعني العلة الغائية؛ لئلا يكون الناظر عابثا. وثانيها المنفعة؛ لتسهل عليه المشقة في تحصيله. وثالثها: التسمية، أعني عنوان العلم؛ ليكون عند الناظر إجمال ما يفصله الغرض.
ورابعها المؤلّف؛ ليسكن قلب المتعلم وخامسها أنه في أي مرتبة هو؛ ليعلم على أيّ علم يجب تقديمه، وعن أيّ علم يجب تأخيره. وسادسها: من أي علم هو؛ ليطلب ما يليق به وسابعها القسمة وهو أبواب العلم والكتاب. وثامنها: أنحاء التعليم وهي التقسيم والتحليل والتحديد والبرهان؛ ليعرف أن الكتاب مشتمل على كلها أو بعضها.
ورابعها المؤلف إلخ: بكسر اللام أي تعيين المؤلّف ومعرفته ليسكن حال المتعلم، ويطمئن قلبه في قبول كلامه بالاعتماد عليه؛ لأن معرفة حال الأقوال بمراتب الرجال. في أي مرتبة هو إلخ كما يقال إن مرتبة المنطق أن يشتغل به بعد تهذيب الأخلاق، وتقويم الفكر ببعض الهندسيات. أيّ علم هو إلخ من أي جنس من أجناس العلوم العقلية أو النقلية، أو الفرعية، أو الأصلية. القسمة إلخ أي قسمة العلم والكتاب. فالأول أي قسمة العلم كما يقال: أبواب المنطق تسعة: الأول الكليات الخمس، الثاني التعريفات الثالث القياس الرابع القضايا وأخواته الخامس البرهان السادس الجدل، السابع الخطابة، الثامن المغالطة، التاسع الشعر. وبعضهم عد بحث الألفاظ بابا آخر؛ فصار أبواب المنطق عشرة كاملة. والثاني أي قسمة الكتاب، فتقسيمه بالأبواب والفصول شائع لا يخلو عنه كتاب كما ترى. وهي التقسيم إلخ التقسيم عبارة عن التكثر من فوق إلى أسفل كتقسيم الجنس إلى الأنواع، والنوع إلى الأصناف، والذاتي إلى الجنس والنوع والفصل والعرض إلى الخاصة والعرض العام والتحليل هو التكثير من السفل إلى فوق والتحديد فعل الحد، وهو يدل على الشيء بما به قوامه دلالة مفصلة والبرهان طريق موثوق به، موصل إلى الوقوف على الحق هذا. وإن شئت مزيد تفصيل في أنحاء التعليم فعليك النظر في كتب الفن.
Section on the eight headings: Know that the ancients used to mention eight things in the introductions of books, and they called them 'the eight headings'. First: the aim (gharad), meaning the final cause, so that the inquirer is not aimless. Second: the benefit (manfa'a), so that the difficulty of obtaining it becomes easy for him. Third: the naming (tasmiya), meaning the title of the science, so that the inquirer has a summary of what the aim details. Fourth: the author (mu'allaf), to calm the heart of the learner. Fifth: what rank it is in, so that he knows which science should precede and which should follow. Sixth: which science it is from, so that he seeks what is suitable for it. Seventh: the division (qisma), which are the sections of the science and the book. Eighth: the modes of instruction (anwā' al-ta'līm), which are division, analysis, definition, and demonstration, so that he knows whether the book contains all or some of them. And the fourth: by breaking the lam, i.e., specifying the author and knowing him so that the learner's condition calms and his heart is at ease in accepting his words by relying on him, because knowing the states of statements is by the ranks of men. 'In what rank it is' etc., as it is said that the rank of logic is that one should occupy himself with it after refining morals and correcting thought with some geometrical matters. 'Which science it is from' etc., from which genus of the genera of sciences: rational or transmitted, subsidiary or original. 'The division' etc., i.e., the division of the science and the book. The first, i.e., the division of the science as it is said: The sections of logic are nine: first the five universals, second definitions, third syllogism, fourth propositions and their sisters, fifth demonstration, sixth dialectic, seventh rhetoric, eighth sophistry, ninth poetry. And some counted the discussion of terms as another section, so the sections of logic became a complete ten. The second, i.e., the division of the book, so its division into chapters and sections is widespread and no book is without it as you see. And they are 'the division' etc. Division is the multiplication from top to bottom, like dividing genus into species, species into varieties, essential into genus, species, difference, accident into property and general accident. Analysis is the multiplication from bottom to top. Definition (hadd) is the act of defining, which indicates something by what constitutes it in detailed indication. Demonstration is a trustworthy method leading to the discovery of truth. This. And if you want more detail on the modes of instruction, then you must look into the books of the art.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
